#### ADVANTAGE 1\-\--ARCTIC SUSTAINABILITY.

#### Arctic emissions accelerate ice melt\-\--That [builds]{.underline} a [catastrophic methane]{.underline} bomb.

**Arndt '24** \[Kyle; September 25; MIT Climate Portal Writing citing
Kyle Arndt, Arctic Research Scientist at the Woodwell Climate Research
Center, PhD in Ecology; MIT Climate Portal, "Is methane release from the
Arctic unstoppable?"
https://climate.mit.edu/ask-mit/methane-release-arctic-unstoppable\]

[**[Under]{.mark}** the [Arctic **ice** lies]{.mark} an **extremely
[carbon-rich]{.mark}** [environment]{.mark}]{.underline}. Over thousands
of years, plants in the Arctic have absorbed carbon dioxide (CO2) from
the air to grow, before being buried under snow and ice during the
winter, becoming part of the soil. If this perennially frozen ground
thaws---as it is now, as a result of climate change---ancient plants are
uncovered, alongside plant-eating microbes that break them down.

[This [releases]{.mark}]{.underline} two main climate-warming greenhouse
gases: [[**CO2**, and]{.underline}]{.mark} even more **[potent
[methane]{.mark}]{.underline}**.

Seasonal thawing in the Arctic is normal. The region has very cold
winters and warmer summers. Every year, the top, "active layer" of soil
thaws and refreezes, exchanging methane and CO2 with the atmosphere. But
today, those methane and CO2 [[emissions]{.mark} have **greatly
[increased]{.mark}**[, as]{.mark} human-caused climate **[warming
causes]{.mark}** the [**active layer** to grow
deeper]{.mark}]{.underline} and deeper, [[digging into]{.mark} the
"**[permafrost]{.mark}**" below]{.underline}. The Arctic is warming four
times faster than the world average,1 and as a direct result, methane
emissions from the Boreal-Arctic region (which includes the Arctic as
well as some areas a bit further south) have increased by 9 percent
since 2002.2 Some areas have become an annual CO2 source, meaning more
CO2 is released during the year than plants can absorb during the short
summers.

[The [**scale** of]{.mark} these [emissions is **so large**]{.mark} that
[they]{.mark} are likely [to]{.mark} create a **[feedback
loop]{.mark}**]{.underline}, says Kyle Arndt, a research scientist
focused on the Arctic at the Woodwell Climate Research Center. The extra
greenhouse gases in the atmosphere will [[make]{.underline}]{.mark} the
[[planet **warm faster**]{.mark}, which then [causes]{.mark} more of the
[**Arctic** to **thaw**]{.mark}]{.underline}.

But this feedback loop is not like an "on or off" switch, which, once
flipped, will eventually release all the carbon buried in the Arctic.
It's more like a dimmer switch, where every fraction of a degree the
planet warms causes more permafrost to thaw. And that means that
everything we do to reduce warming---by choosing clean transportation
and electricity, for instance, instead of those run on climate-warming
fossil fuels---will keep more permafrost stable and frozen. "Any amount
of reduced, human-caused emissions that we can do means reduced
emissions from the Arctic," says Arndt.

Exactly how much methane release we can prevent is hard to say.
Scientists understand very well how methane is released from the Arctic,
but they are still working out how fast it may happen. Thawing into the
permafrost "is on a one-way trajectory right now," says Arndt. "We do
expect to lose a good amount of the permafrost area by the end of the
century due to warming."

Arndt says this [**thawing [permafrost]{.mark}** is often [**not
accounted** for in **global**]{.mark} **climate
[models]{.mark}**]{.underline}, which means climate plans based on these
models may underestimate future warming. Scientists are also working to
better understand a phenomenon called "**[[abrupt]{.mark}
thaw]{.underline}**," where [[permafrost thaws]{.mark} **very
[quickly]{.mark}**]{.underline}. That [[leads to]{.mark} **landslides**
and [ground **collapse**]{.mark}]{.underline}, as ice suddenly turns to
water, [[releasing **large**]{.mark} **amounts** of **[methane]{.mark}**
suddenly]{.underline}. Arndt says scientists still can't predict how
much this will happen or exactly where.

But we can be sure that, if we do nothing more to halt climate change,
the [**[Arctic]{.mark}** will become a **gigantic** source of
**g**reen**h**ouse **g**ases]{.underline} over the next century, says
Arndt. At current rates of warming, the region, which includes part of
Russia, Greenland, Canada, Scandinavian countries, and Alaska, [is [on
**pace** to **contribute**]{.mark} as much [**methane** and
**CO2**]{.mark} to the atmosphere [as a **large**, **industrialized**
country]{.mark}]{.underline}.3

#### Black carbon in the Arctic is [especially detrimental]{.underline} to global warming -- the tipping point is [now]{.underline}. 

Dr Sian **Prior'3-31** - Lead Adviser to the Clean Arctic Alliance with
over 25 years of experience (\"Black Carbon and the Arctic: What is
Happening, and What Comes Next?\", Clean Arctic Alliance,
https://cleanarctic.org/2025/03/31/black-carbon-and-the-arctic-what-is-happening-and-what-comes-next/,
3-31-2025, Accessed 6-28-2025)//Ilake-EO

The [Arctic]{.underline} is [warming **four times faster**
than]{.underline} the [rest of the planet]{.underline}, and rapidly
[approaching the point where]{.underline} such as the [**loss of sea
ice** and melting of the Greenland ice sheet, are affecting ocean
circulation patterns in the North Atlantic.]{.underline}

[**Black carbon** is solid particulate matter]{.underline} -- which we
call soot -- [produced by]{.underline} the [incomplete burning of fossil
fuels in]{.underline} the [engine combustion chambers]{.underline} and
[then ejected into the]{.underline} [atmosphere]{.underline}. This [soot
absorbs sunlight and]{.underline} by [warm]{.underline}ing the
[surrounding atmosphere]{.underline} [becomes a]{.underline} powerful
short-lived climate pollutant, aka **[super pollutant]{.underline}**.
Although black carbon is not a greenhouse gas, these [emissions form
"shipping's second largest **cause of global warming**]{.underline}"
after carbon dioxide, and makes up around one-fifth of international
shipping's already considerable climate impact.

Overall, [black carbon contributes more than **three thousand times**
the climate warming impact of]{.underline} the same mass of [CO2
i]{.underline}n the atmosphere over a 20 year period. In its recent 6th
Assessment Report, the UN Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change
[(IPCC) doubled]{.underline} the [estimate of the warming potential of
black carbon on **snow and ice**]{.underline} compared with the 5th
Assessment Report due to a better understanding of its impact on snow
forcing.

After they are emitted from ship exhausts, black carbon particles are
suspended in the atmosphere, where they absorb heat from the sun.
[When]{.underline} the [particles fall to]{.underline} earth -- or in
this case, [the Arctic]{.underline}, this black soot falls onto white
ice or snow, where [it lowers the]{.underline} reflectivity -- or
[albedo]{.underline}, [causing a **detrimental absorption**
of]{.underline} more [heat and]{.underline} thus **[accelerates
melting]{.underline}**. The **[feedback loop]{.underline}**
[continues]{.underline} [when the snow or ice melts]{.underline},
exposing darker areas of land and water which absorb further heat from
the sun, thus [reducing the reflective capacity of the planet's polar
ice cap.]{.underline}

[More heat in the polar systems equals **increased
melting**]{.underline}. While this seems obvious, scientists are seeing
ever more clearly the alarming consequences.. [Declines in sea ice
extent and volume]{.underline} are [lead]{.underline}ing
[to]{.underline} a burgeoning [social and environmental
crisis]{.underline} in the Arctic, while cascading changes are impacting
global climate and ocean circulation. [Scientists have]{.underline}
[high confidence]{.underline} that [processes]{.underline} are [nearing
points beyond]{.underline} which [**rapid and irreversible changes**
with serious implications for humanity]{.underline}, and research shows
[within the next decade]{.underline} we will start to [see periods when
no Arctic summer sea ice is formed]{.underline}, and that "[preparations
need to be made for]{.underline} the [increased **extreme weather**
across the northern hemisphere]{.underline} that is likely to occur as a
result."

#### Reject NEG [impact defense]{.underline}\-\--theirs [models]{.underline} don't factor [permafrost]{.underline} feedback-loops or [uncertainty]{.underline}.

**Holdren '24** \[John and a summary of a panel of climate scientists;
June 11; former co-director of the Office of Science and Technology
Policy in the Obama administration and now a research professor at
Harvard University's Kennedy School of Government; The Salata Institute
for Climate and Sustainability at Harvard University, "Thawing
permafrost: what does it mean? And what can be done?"
https://salatainstitute.harvard.edu/thawing-permafrost-what-does-it-mean-and-what-can-be-done/\]

[One of the **[biggest]{.mark} remaining
[unknowns]{.mark}**]{.underline} [[in modeling]{.underline}]{.mark} the
effects of [climate change [is]{.mark} the]{.underline} impact of
[**thawing [permafrost]{.mark}** in the Arctic]{.underline} region,
[[which]{.mark} could [unleash **large quantities** of]{.mark}
climate-**warming [methane]{.mark}**]{.underline}. While this has been
known for decades, [[there is **so much**
uncertainty]{.underline}]{.mark} about the details [[that]{.mark}
present **climate [models]{.mark}** [do not include]{.mark} this effect
-- [and]{.mark} thus could be **seriously [underestimating]{.mark}** the
**[rate]{.mark}** of global warming, [and]{.mark}]{.underline} the
[**[intensity]{.mark}** of measures]{.underline} needed to control it.

Experts spoke about this issue in a June 11 Harvard Climate Action Week
panel moderated by Henry Lee, the Jassim M. Jaidah Family Director of
the Environment and Natural Resources Program with the Belfer Center at
Harvard Kennedy School. To open the discussion, Lee asked the panel "Why
should anyone living in the temperate areas in our planet worry about
permafrost thaw in the Arctic?"

John Holdren, former co-director of the Office of Science and Technology
Policy in the Obama administration and now a research professor at
Harvard University's Kennedy School of Government, answered that "one
reason is that it is the emissions from society's activities in the
temperate regions, above all, that are driving global climate change.
And global climate change is happening three to four times faster in the
Arctic than in the world on average. And if the world doesn't slow down
its emissions at a greater rate that is happening today, all that is
going to get worse."

What's more, Holdren added, "[there are **[feedbacks]{.mark}** from it
getting **worse faster** in the Arctic, which]{.underline} in turn [will
[**accelerate** climate]{.mark} change [worldwide]{.mark}]{.underline}."
The [[feedbacks]{.mark} [have]{.mark} to do with **[vast
amounts]{.mark}**]{.underline} -- the [exact [extent]{.mark} of [which
are **unknown**]{.mark}]{.underline} -- [of **[methane]{.mark}** gas
that [is **frozen** into]{.mark} the **Arctic [permafrost]{.mark}**.
As]{.underline} the global climate warms up, [this permafrost [**thaws**
and releases]{.mark}]{.underline}, in addition to [**[carbon]{.mark}**
dioxide, **[methane]{.mark}**]{.underline}, a potent if short-lived
greenhouse gas. [This [causes]{.mark} even **more [warming]{.mark}**,
thus **[thawing more]{.mark}** permafrost [and **releasing**]{.mark}
**even [more]{.mark}** methane, and **so on**]{.underline}.

#### Newest [studies concur]{.underline}\-\--an Arctic bomb [surpasses]{.underline} historical [peaks]{.underline}. 

**Ralls '24** \[Eric, citing a Dartmouth Study led by Joanmarrie

could **[release]{.mark}**]{.underline} between 22 billion and [[**432

to]{.underline} as much as [[**550 billion** tons, **juxtaposed**

#### Arctic sustainability is controlling\-\--it preserves global marine health\-\--extinction. 

**Carmack '19** \[Eddy, Jeremy Wilkinson, Jan-Gunnar Winther, and Gail
Whiteman; January 9; Senior Research Scientist Emeritus for the
Department of Fisheries and Oceans, Institute of Ocean Sciences in
Sidney, British Columbia.; Sea Ice Physicist, British Antarctic Survey;
Director, National Centre for the Ocean and the Arctic, Norway;
Professor of Sustainability, University of Exeter Business School,
University of Exeter; World Economic Forum, "4 reasons why the Arctic is
key to our planet's survival,"
https://www.weforum.org/stories/2019/01/4-reasons-why-the-arctic-is-key-to-our-planets-survival/\]

While our planet contains five oceans - the Atlantic, Pacific, Indian,
Arctic and Southern (Antarctic) - it is the [Arctic Ocean]{.underline}
that [is]{.underline} **[most susceptible]{.underline}** [to climate
change]{.underline}. In fact these changes are happening in front of our
eyes; we have lost over half the Arctic summer sea ice already.

There are four reasons why [the]{.underline} **[[Arctic]{.mark}
Ocean]{.underline}** [is]{.underline} [distinct]{.mark} from other
oceans and [**[critical]{.underline}** [to]{.underline}
]{.mark}[our]{.underline} **[planet's [survival]{.mark}]{.underline}**:

First, while this relatively small (by ocean standards) marine
environment holds only 1% of the world's ocean volume and occupies only
3% of the world's ocean surface area, [its [impact on]{.mark}
the]{.underline} **[global [climate]{.mark} system]{.underline}**
[[is]{.underline}]{.mark} **[disproportionately]{.underline}**
[[large]{.underline}]{.mark}.

Second, though small in area, [it scoops up over]{.underline}
**[10%]{.underline}** [of]{.underline} global [river runoff and claims
twenty of the world's 100 longest rivers]{.underline}.

Third, [the Arctic marine domain comprises about a]{.underline}
**[third]{.underline}** [of the world's coastline]{.underline}.

And fourth, [the Arctic Ocean contains]{.underline}
**[one-quarter]{.underline}** [of the world's continental shelf,
of]{.underline} **[immense]{.underline}** [socio-ecological
importance]{.underline}.

On all counts, the little Arctic Ocean holds its own on the global
stage.

Despite its importance, the Arctic Ocean sometimes goes missing from
public ocean-talk as more 'local' marine issues take centre stage. A key
point for non-experts is to recognize that the [[Arctic]{.mark} Ocean
[is]{.mark}]{.underline} different from other oceans and it is
[[the]{.underline}]{.mark} **[critical [driver]{.mark}]{.underline}**
[[of the]{.mark} global]{.underline} **[[oceanic conveyor
belt]{.underline}]{.mark}**. For example, [the [**Gulf Stream** and
**North Atlantic Current**]{.mark}]{.underline}
[[are]{.underline}]{.mark} strongly [[regulated by]{.underline}]{.mark}
processes that occur in [the [Arctic]{.mark}]{.underline}. The Arctic
Ocean is remote to many of us, but its influence can be felt everywhere.

How do we know this? [Oceanographers have robust]{.underline}
**[scientific evidence]{.underline}** [on the role the Arctic plays
within our planet]{.underline}. For instance, the global hydrological
cycle determines the distribution of water around the planet -- and
affects food and water security globally. This cycle begins in warmer
climates, with the equatorial trade winds and mid-latitude westerlies.
It is refined and shaped by the global distribution of land masses that,
in the northern hemisphere, collect precipitation and direct it poleward
through massive rivers. These [rivers introduce]{.underline} substantial
amounts of [fresh water into the]{.underline} upper layers of the
[Arctic Ocean]{.underline}, providing the required conditions for an ice
cover to form, and [[regulating]{.underline}
**[nutrient]{.underline}**]{.mark} **[supplies]{.underline}**
[[and]{.underline} **[biological productivity]{.underline}**]{.mark}.

But [these dynamics are undergoing]{.underline}
**[unprecedented]{.underline}** and worrying [changes]{.underline} that
are already visible. Jonathan Smith, producer of Blue Planet II,
reflected on the new realities of filming in the Arctic Ocean: "We were
all set and ready to film but we needed two major things -- walrus and
ice. I had expected that walrus may be hard to find, but I did not
expect it to be hard to find ice in the Arctic.... the crew were all
commenting how surprisingly warm it was." They were not mistaken. Over
many years, scientists have been recording changes in global
temperature, hydrological cycles and sea-ice cover. These changes have
all had significant and eminently observable effects on the Arctic
Ocean. Over the past two decades alone, [summer sea-ice
coverage]{.underline} in the Arctic Ocean has [decreased by]{.underline}
about **[seven million]{.underline}** [square kilometres to
just]{.underline} over **[3 million]{.underline}** [square
kilometres]{.underline}. This loss represents a surface area larger than
all but about a dozen countries in the world.

However, [the [Arctic]{.mark} Ocean]{.underline} is not just a passive
victim of anthropogenic change: it also [[drives]{.underline}
**[disruptions]{.underline}** [back to the]{.underline} **[global
system]{.underline}**]{.mark} in ways that were overlooked even a decade
ago. Though the exact mechanisms are still debated, [Arctic sea-[ice
loss]{.mark}]{.underline} may be [[affect]{.underline}]{.mark}ing both
[ocean]{.underline} **[[currents]{.underline}]{.mark}**
[[and]{.underline}]{.mark} mid-latitude **[[weather]{.mark}
patterns]{.underline}** along our southern borders. And the processes
occurring in the Arctic play a vital role in connecting the Pacific and
the Atlantic. The fabled Northwest Passage is more than a destination
for explorers and commercial ship traffic; [the Arctic is an
oceanographic freight train that]{.underline} **[ties
together]{.underline}** [and influences other maritime regions and the
global **climate system**]{.underline}.

Worryingly [there are big [changes in Arctic]{.mark}]{.underline}
sea[-[ice]{.mark} cover]{.underline}, **[freshwater
supply]{.underline}**, **[nutrient]{.underline}** [availability
[and]{.mark}]{.underline} the **[underwater]{.underline}** light
**[[climate]{.underline}]{.mark}** which are [taking place at
an]{.underline} [**accelerated pace**, [with]{.mark} associated [shifts
in]{.mark}]{.underline} **[seawater [chemistry]{.mark}]{.underline}**
[and]{.underline} [**biological activity**, and alterations
to]{.underline} [**[habitat]{.underline}** [and]{.underline}
**[food-web]{.underline}**]{.mark} [structure that are
[affect]{.mark}ing [the]{.mark}]{.underline} [**[entire
ocean]{.underline}**]{.mark}. [Ocean [waters are
growing]{.mark}]{.underline} [[**more acidic**]{.mark}, and]{.underline}
**[[oxygen]{.mark} concentrations]{.underline}** have [decreased by
nearly]{.underline} **[8%]{.underline}** over the past 50 years alone.
From this perspective, it seems inevitable that the ongoing and rapid
[changes in the]{.underline} physical environment of the marine [Arctic
[will push]{.mark} existing]{.underline} [socio-[ecological
systems]{.mark}]{.underline} - small and large - **[[beyond tipping
points]{.underline}]{.mark}** and into new regimes.

What this means is that [temperature, pH and oxygen changes [lead
to]{.mark}]{.underline} [**species [extinctions]{.mark}**[, threatening
**fisheries** and **food security**]{.mark}]{.underline}. In addition,
the National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration recently issued a
new report card on the health of the Arctic ocean, which conlduded: "The
report card calls marine microplastics an 'emerging threat' in the
Arctic, and it notes that a recent global survey shows that the Arctic
Ocean has higher concentrations of microplastics than any other ocean
basin in the world." When German scientists found 11,000 particles of
microplastic in one litre of melted sea ice from the remote Arctic,
everyone began to understand how widespread and serious this is.
Unbelievable as it may sound, the plastic soup has now reached Arctic
waters.

#### Melting Arctic ice unearths new zombie viruses that humans lack defenses against. 

**Doyle '24** \[Liam citing Jean-Michel Claverie; January 21st; News
reporter at Express.co.uk; Emeritus Professor at the School of Medicine

[could]{.mark}]{.underline} [**[fuel]{.underline}**

[[next]{.underline} **[global]{.underline}**]{.mark}

[off]{.underline}.

Scientists led by Professor Claverie in 2014 found that
[these]{.underline} \"**[[zombie]{.underline}]{.mark}**\" [Siberian
[viruses could]{.mark}]{.underline} []{.mark}still

**[[organisms]{.underline}]{.mark}**.

#### Arctic SMR deployment is key to arctic sustainability. 

**Nesheiwat '21** \[Julia; July 22; Distinguished Fellow at the Atlantic
Council Global Energy Center, Commissioner on the US Arctic Research
Commission, PhD from the Tokyo Institute of Technology, former homeland
security advisor; Arctic Council, "Expanding nuclear energy to the
Arctic: The potential of small modular reactors,"
https://www.atlanticcouncil.org/blogs/energysource/expanding-nuclear-energy-to-the-arctic-the-potential-of-small-modular-reactors/\]

With increased emphasis on achieving political and technological
solutions to climate change, many **[experts]{.underline}** in the
global community [are turning]{.underline} their focus [to]{.underline}
the virtually **[emissions-free]{.underline}** [power produced
by]{.underline} **[nuclear]{.underline}** [reactors]{.underline}. The
continued development of small modular reactors (**[SMRs]{.underline}**)
[offers]{.underline} a [potential]{.underline} opportunity [to
overcome]{.underline} many of the [hindrances presented by]{.underline}
**[larger]{.underline}** nuclear power [plants, including]{.underline}
high **[costs]{.underline}**, [complex]{.underline} [**supply chains**,
large]{.underline} physical [**infrastructure**, and]{.underline}
**[unsuitability]{.underline}** [in harsh]{.underline}
[**environments**, such as the]{.underline} **[Arctic]{.underline}**.
The Biden Administration and UK Prime Minister Boris Johnson have both
signaled that SMRs have a key role in combatting carbon emissions and
meeting increased energy demand. More recently, several Canadian
provincial governments have joined forces with private corporations to
conduct feasibility studies for the deployment of SMRs in the far north.
**[SMRs]{.underline}** [present a]{.underline} **[vital]{.underline}**
[opportunity for]{.underline} **[energy]{.underline}** [production in
the Arctic, where energy]{.underline} **[needs]{.underline}**
[are]{.underline} **[more demanding]{.underline}** [and]{.underline}
**[difficult]{.underline}** to meet [due to]{.underline} the region's
[**low temperatures**, low]{.underline} **[population]{.underline}**
density, [and]{.underline} **[inaccessibility]{.underline}**. While
other energy sources such as natural [gas and oil are]{.underline}
abundant in the Arctic, their exploitation is [not]{.underline}
**[sustainable]{.underline}** [and]{.underline} often [comes at
a]{.underline} **[high cost]{.underline}** to local communities and the
environment. While nuclear energy is not a new technology, recent
advancements like SMRs present a promising solution to challenges
associated with traditional nuclear power and fossil fuels, as well as
along with high-polluting diesel generators used in the Arctic.

Arctic nations have explored the viability of nuclear energy production
in the region for years. Despite high electricity production
capabilities, [traditional nuclear]{.underline} power plants [entail
**complicated**]{.underline} waste disposal [operations and
have]{.underline} more recently [come under]{.underline} increased
**[scrutiny]{.underline}** over safety concerns and broader public
perception issues, especially in Europe. In contrast, [SMRs
are]{.underline} **[smaller]{.underline}**, [easily deployed
in]{.underline} almost [**any environment**, and feature]{.underline}
**[extensive]{.underline}** [passive]{.underline} [**safety features**.
They also use a set design framework and have extended]{.underline}
[**fuel cycles**, which reduces]{.underline}
**[challenges]{.underline}** [associated with]{.underline}
**[spent]{.underline}** nuclear **[fuel]{.underline}** [and]{.underline}
**[lowers]{.underline}** the [likelihood of]{.underline}
**[radiation]{.underline}** escaping containment.

[The Arctic]{.underline} currently [suffers from a]{.underline}
**[dearth]{.underline}** [of]{.underline} **[renewable]{.underline}**
[electricity generation, despite]{.underline} **[copious
opportunities]{.underline}**. Roughly **[half]{.underline}** [of
the]{.underline} [Arctic's populations live in]{.underline} [**remote
locations**, requiring]{.underline} **[off-grid]{.underline}**
[systems]{.underline}; consequently, approximately 80 percent of Arctic
communities depend exclusively on diesel generators for electricity.
Despite the region's abundance of natural resources, most diesel [fuel
is]{.underline} [**imported**, threatening]{.underline} **[energy
security]{.underline}** [with]{.underline} both increased
**[costs]{.underline}** [and]{.underline} the likelihood of
**[shortages]{.underline}**. While wind, solar, and hydropower are also
viable green alternatives to fossil fuels, each comes with its own
disadvantages in comparison to SMRs. **[Wind]{.underline}** [turbines
are]{.underline} **[not]{.underline}** yet [adapted to]{.underline} the
**[harsh]{.underline}** [Arctic **conditions**]{.underline} [with
persistent temperatures below]{.underline} -- **[forty
degrees]{.underline}** [Celsius]{.underline}. They likewise carry an

especially migratory birds. **[Solar]{.underline}** energy systems also
[require]{.underline} **[extensive land]{.underline}** to reach higher

**[uranium]{.underline}** ([which is]{.underline}
**[abundant]{.underline}** [in the Arctic), and]{.underline}
**[minor]{.underline}** environmental **[footprint]{.underline}**.

Nevertheless, installing SMRs in the Arctic comes with its own set of
unique challenges. SMRs for commercial use are currently restricted by
regulatory regimes that are far more accustomed to licensing
conventional nuclear power plants. [SMRs]{.underline} also generate less
power than conventional nuclear plants and require a connection to the

**[costs]{.underline}**, [which could]{.underline} in turn [be used to

to]{.underline} more **[remote]{.underline}** [settlements]{.underline}.

As a result of the 2019 National Defense Authorization Act (NDAA), the
Pentagon initiated Project Pele which commissioned two companies to
design SMRs for its remote operating bases. The research and development
project required the companies design and build a one to five megawatt
reactor that can last at least three years and be rapidly redeployed and
removed. The project---under which BWXT Advanced Technologies and
X-Energy will develop prototypes to be evaluated by the Pentagon---has
the potential to pave the way toward the adoption of transportable
microreactors critical to supplying clean, affordable power to remote
communities with limited access to an electrical grid. The Pentagon
plans to make a full evaluation in 2022 to determine whether to proceed
to limited production.

#### SCENARIO 2\-\--ENERGY TRANSITION:

#### SMR's are the [future of nuclear energy]{.underline} -- enabling [global transition]{.underline}. 

Najmedin **Meshkati'4-22** - Professor of Civil and Environmental
Engineering, recipient of the Presidential Young Investigator Award from
the National Science Foundation, and Principal Investigator of a
research grant from the Nuclear Regulatory Commission (\"Is nuclear
power the key to a low-carbon future?\", USC Today,
https://today.usc.edu/is-nuclear-power-the-key-to-a-low-carbon-future/,
4-22-2025, Accessed 6-28-2025)//Ilake-EO

[Nuclear energy]{.underline} stands [poised to serve as a **cornerstone
of the global transition** to a low-carbon future]{.underline}. Unlike
intermittent renewable sources such as wind and solar, [nuclear power
provides **reliable**, large-scale baseload electricity]{.underline}
[that can **stabilize energy grids** and complement clean energy
portfolios]{.underline}. Recent policy and industry developments
underscore this renewed momentum.

By 2050, nuclear energy capacity is expected to triple globally to meet
net-zero emissions targets, as pledged by 22 countries at COP28 in Dubai
in December 2023. Since the initial pledge at COP28, six additional
nations have joined the declaration at COP29 in November 2024. The
planned restart of the Palisades nuclear power plant in Michigan, which
was shut down in 2022, and Unit 1 of the Three Mile Island nuclear power
plant, which was not involved in the 1979 accident but was shut down in
2019 due to economic challenges, is expected by mid-2028. Reports
indicate that the Duane Arnold nuclear plant in Iowa, which ceased
operations in 2020, may also pursue a restart.

This unprecedented trend would mark the first time in U.S. history that
decommissioned [nuclear plants are]{.underline} brought back online,
[enabled by **federal and bipartisan** political support]{.underline}.
According to recent reports, due to the unexpectedly slow expansion of
renewable energy in Germany, [there is a vocal and growing movement to
restart]{.underline} some of the country's 11 shutdown [nuclear power
reactors]{.underline}.

Emerging nuclear technologies offer promising pathways for overcoming
many legacy challenges associated with traditional nuclear energy.
[Small modular reactors]{.underline} **[(SMRs)]{.underline}** promise to
[reduce costs,]{.underline} construction timelines and land use while
[enhanc]{.underline}ing [safety features through passive cooling and
underground containment]{.underline}. Additionally, advanced reactor
designs --- such as molten salt, gas-cooled and thorium-fueled systems
--- are being developed for greater efficiency and reduced waste
generation. [These innovations]{.underline} could **[revolutionize the
energy landscape]{.underline}** in the coming decades [by providing
nearly limitless, **carbon-free power** with minimal safety and waste
concerns]{.underline}.

1\. Introduction

The [[Arctic]{.mark} region['s **socioeconomic diversity** and]{.mark}
**[varying]{.mark} [development]{.mark} strategies** among Arctic states

**[low-carbon energy]{.mark}** solutions]{.underline}, a transition
reliant on the presence of robust industrialisation and ground
infrastructure. However, [a critical [factor that complicates]{.mark}
this transition [is]{.mark} the [**off-grid nature** of]{.mark} many
[Arctic]{.mark} [communities]{.mark}.]{.underline}1 [Despite existing
challenges, there is **[growing interest]{.mark}** in renewable and

by]{.underline} the [dual objectives [of achieving **economic savings**
and **safeguarding**]{.mark} **[the environment]{.mark}**.]{.underline}
Recognising the pivotal role of local perspectives in the formulation of
sustainable energy policies, [it is essential to acknowledge the need to
tailor these policies to address the unique needs and circumstances of
Arctic communities.]{.underline} In this context, both renewable and
[**[nuclear energy solutions]{.mark}** are increasingly being considered
as [part of]{.mark} the transition towards [achieving **net-zero
emissions**]{.mark}]{.underline}. Applying the context of the energy
transition towards net-zero in Canada, this study examines how nuclear
energy development projects in the Arctic may affect the environment and

[Small Modular Reactors (**[SMRs]{.mark}**]{.underline})
[[are]{.underline}]{.mark} increasingly being [viewed as part of
**low-carbon energy transition** in a climate change [solution]{.mark},

they]{.mark} can [provide a **stable energy load**]{.mark}]{.underline}.
This article explores the role of SMRs as part of an energy transition,
using Canada as a case setting. Canada's proactive adoption of SMRs as a
component of its strategy for decarbonisation is substantiated by its
well-established nuclear supply chain complemented by robust technical
and production capabilities.3Significantly, Canada's relevance in the
Arctic context is underscored by its Indigenous population, comprising
First Nations, Métis, and Inuit communities. This study employs
qualitative data from public sources, including government publications,
Indigenous-led organisations' media, and webinar transcripts, to explore
stakeholder views on SMRs in Canada, particularly in the context of
Arctic energy transitions. It is essential to note that this research
aims to gauge publicly available vocal perspectives surrounding SMRs in
Canada, and not to speak on behalf of Indigenous communities.

### Solvency\-\--Arctic Black Carbon\-\--2AC

#### [Small modular reactors]{.underline} in the [Arctic]{.underline} are [key to]{.underline} mitigating the [impacts]{.underline} of black carbon. 

- This is the most sus card in the world

Thad W. **Allen et al'17** (Christine Todd Whitman, Esther Brimmer;
"Arctic Imperatives: Reinforcing U.S. Strategy on America's Fourth
Coast. Council on Foreign Relations," JSTOR,
http://www.jstor.org/stable/resrep21840, 1-1-2017, Accessed
6-27-2025)//Ilake-EO

In recent years, U.S. Arctic strategy has included clean energy
measures, such as increased solar and wind generation, to displace some
diesel demand in remote communities. The Task Force supports these
measures but is concerned that implementation and funding will lessen
over time. Although it would take years to recoup the investment costs,
this burden is an inherent part of shifting to a more sustainable
economic platform. Further, Alaska has become a leader in developing
innovative, off-grid renewable energy technologies---the Alaska Center
for Energy and Power and the Alaska Renewable Energy Project being prime
examples---and could become an exporter of knowledge and know-how. The
Task Force finds that [the Arctic could serve as an **incubator for
energy innovation**]{.underline}. [Greater use of renewables would
reduce dependence on **costly diesel fuel**]{.underline},
[which]{.underline} [produces harmful emissions]{.underline} and has
detrimental effects on health. [New technological developments in
nuclear energy]{.underline}, [such as **small modular
reactors**]{.underline}, [also offer an opportunity]{.underline} [for
**economic development** in the region while **reducing dependence on
fossil fuels**]{.underline}. ENVIRONMENTAL CONCERNS The Arctic is a
remarkable region, encompassing vast areas of intact marine and
terrestrial habitats, some of the roughest seas in the world, an extreme
and variable climate, diverse human cultures whose traditions are
closely tied to the bounty of the land and sea, and abundant wildlife.
The rich Arctic environment attracts significant wildlife to the region,
including millions of birds from every continent each year, bowhead
whales from the Bering Sea, and gray whales from Mexico. As the Arctic
sea ice declines, resources that were once remote and difficult to
access are becoming increasingly accessible, opening not only the way to
new commerce but also new risks. For instance, ship hulls and holds, in
addition to sewage and other discharge from these vessels, can carry
invasive species into Arctic waters. In 2004, member states adopted the
IMO's International Convention for the Control and Management of Ships'
Ballast Water and Sediments. Having received sufficient ratifications,
the measure enters into force on September 8, 2017. Additionally, noise
from passing ships can disorient marine mammals and make them change
their routes. Changing migration patterns, in turn, affect the animals
and the Alaska Natives who harvest them. As economic activity in the
region increases, the Task Force finds that [the United States will need
**sustained policies** supported by scientific data]{.underline} and
evaluation to support culturally and environmentally sensitive economic
development. The sea is a major focus of environmental conservation
efforts, but the air in the Arctic matters as well. Thawing permafrost
releases carbon and methane, both greenhouse gases. A pound of methane
can trap twenty-five to thirty-three times more heat than a pound of
carbon dioxide.57 Norway has already developed technology for methane
capture, and the United States should follow its example and invest in
state-of-the-art methane capture technology as well. In 2015, [the
Arctic Council created an experts group to monitor]{.underline}
implementation of the council's framework for [**action on black
carbon** and methane]{.underline} by its members. The Task Force
recommends [that Arctic Council members urge]{.underline} observer
[states to **adopt the framework**]{.underline}.58 At some oil and gas
production sites across the world, natural gas is burned off or
"flared," releasing more than three hundred million tons of carbon
dioxide annually, as well as black carbon, into the atmosphere.59 The
Task Force supports the World Bank's Zero Routine Flaring by 2030
initiative, which brings together governments, oil companies, and NGOs
and calls for natural gas to be conserved or used but not wasted by
flaring.

### Internal\-\--Climate Change\-\--2AC

#### Black carbon effects are [unmitigated]{.underline} -- results in [unrestrained accelerated climate change]{.underline} and [irreversible]{.underline} tipping points. 

Daniel **McDougall'23** ("THE CASE FOR ACTION ON BLACK CARBON Avoiding

**[Black carbon]{.underline}**, a pollutant [emitted from the incomplete
combustion of fossil fuels, biomass and waste, sits]{.underline}
uniquely [at the centre of **climate and health crises**]{.underline}.
Black carbon [emissions affect **global warming**, snow and ice
melt,]{.underline} [monsoon and weather patterns]{.underline}, [flood
risk, heat stress]{.underline} and [public health]{.underline}. The
potential [benefits of reducing]{.underline} the [emissions]{.underline}
are [felt]{.underline} most [strongly close to the source]{.underline}.
The dual nature of black carbon as a climate and air pollutant has
[resulted in neither of these fields taking full ownership]{.underline}.
Therefore, [black carbon remains]{.underline} largely **[absent from
mainstream climate and health agendas]{.underline}**.

[Reducing black carbon emissions]{.underline} is [**critically
important** for fast climate mitigation]{.underline} and [**building
resilience** to the effects of climate change]{.underline}. The most
significant [effects of black carbon]{.underline} emissions are [on
sensitive cryosphere ecosystems that are subject to **irreversible
climate tipping points**]{.underline}. [Black carbon deposition on snow
and ice accelerates]{.underline} the [melting of]{.underline} the
[Arctic]{.underline}, the Himalayan and the Andean glaciers as well as
other cryosphere regions. Midlatitude black carbon emissions warm the
surrounding air, which is then transported poleward and contributes to
Arctic warming. Black carbon emissions in Asia and Africa have
contributed to more unpredictable monsoons that decrease water and
energy security, reduce agriculture productivity and cause catastrophic
flooding. Black carbon and other dark aerosols also worsen the impact of
heatwaves, which are getting more frequent in this warming climate

#### Action on black carbon is [lacking]{.underline} 

Daniel **McDougall'23** ("THE CASE FOR ACTION ON BLACK CARBON Avoiding
climate tipping points, building resilience and delivering clean air,"
https://s40026.pcdn.co/wp-content/uploads/Case-for-Action-on-Black-Carbon-CAF-updated-2.pdf,
Clean Air Fund, CSTEP, Berkeley Air Monitoring Group, Orbis Air,
12-2023, Accessed 6-28-2025)//Ilake-EO

Climate policy has emphasised action on carbon dioxide (CO2) and other
well-mixed greenhouse gases that are vital climate warming species, and
these species are the focus of international target setting and
agreements. Conversely, [attention on black carbon has
**lagged**]{.underline}. Only a [small number of countries have
incorporated black carbon targets]{.underline} within their nationally
determined contributions (NDCs), and [black carbon is **not part** of
any climate protocol]{.underline}, [revealing a **significant gap** in
climate strategy]{.underline}. On the other hand, air quality guidelines
focus on PM2.5, nitrogen oxides (NOx), sulphur dioxide (SO2) and other
pollutants to reduce air pollution. PM2.5 is composed of many species,
primarily sulphates, nitrates, organics, black carbon and dust. Thus,
reductions in NOx and SO2 can lead to reductions in PM2.5, and this has
been the focus of many successful air quality interventions (for
example, fuel sulphur standards). The World Health Organization has
highlighted the potential impact of black carbon on health and calls for
more studies but has not set guidelines for black carbon, and so
national and regional air quality standards do not specifically target
black carbon. Regional compacts, such as the Gothenburg Protocol,
acknowledge black carbon as a crucial constituent of PM2.5 but do not
mandate emissions reporting, and its applicability is geographically
confined.i Therefore, [despite its **role in climate
change**,]{.underline} awareness, funding and [action on black carbon
has **lagged behind** other pollutant species]{.underline}. Scientific
complexity has caused uncertainty and stagnation, meaning specific and
widespread action on black carbon in the form of targeted regulation and
mitigation actions has not materialised. Meanwhile, [black carbon
emissions are rising]{.underline} in many places [and]{.underline} are
[not diminishing quickly enough in others]{.underline}. This is [despite
the **ready availability of fuels and technologies** that can rapidly
reduce black carbon emissions;]{.underline} it is technically feasible
to reduce global emissions by 70% by 2030 from 2016 levels.1 The need
for mitigation is immediate and urgent. Hence, this policy brief: •
Provides an update on the latest scientific understanding regarding
black carbon and its role in climate change, public health and
resilience. • Highlights practical solutions and strategies to reduce or
eliminate black carbon emissions. • Outlines a set of targeted
recommendations for governments and funding bodies to ensure fast and
widespread adoption of interventions to reduce black carbon emissions. •
Calls for more research to reduce remaining uncertainties in our
understanding of the multifaceted impacts of black carbon

https://arctic-council.org/about/task-expert/egbcm/, 7-9-2018, Accessed
6-28-2025)//Ilake-EO

[**Black carbon** and methane are]{.underline} short-lived [climate
pollutants that are contributing to **atmospheric
warming**]{.underline}. In addition, [black carbon that falls on snow
and ice accelerates]{.underline} the melting of these reflective
surfaces --consequently accelerating [the impacts of **global warming in
the Arctic**]{.underline}. [Black carbon]{.underline} and methane
[emissions]{.underline} also [contribute directly to air pollution that
harms human health.]{.underline}

[Arctic States are **uniquely positioned** to slow Arctic warming caused
by **emissions of black carbon**]{.underline}. Despite generating just

Arctic]{.underline}, due to the greater warming impact of local emission

3.5.4. Environmental

[The [**absence** of lit]{.mark}erature related to the environmental
impacts [of SMRs is **notable**]{.mark}]{.underline} in this regard.
This may be [due to the obvious [**lack** of an operating plant that can
be a subject for **study**]{.mark}]{.underline}. For comparison's sake,
we may cite the study of Wang et al. (2019), who conducted a life cycle
analysis, including examinations of acidification potential,
eutrophication potential, photochemical ozone creation potential, and
human toxicity potential of wind energy, nuclear power (large reactor),
and hydropower. The study showed that wind power had the highest global
warming potential at 28.6 ± 3.2 g CO2-eq/kWh, followed by nuclear power
at 12.4 ± 1.5 g CO2-eq/kWh and lastly, hydropower at 3.5 ± 0.4 g
CO2-eq/kWh \[87\]. Boldon et al. (2015) conducted a similar study for
wind power and SMRs, noting that they fall within the range for an LR
\[66\]. Moreover, Carless et al. (2016) showed that the GHG emissions of
SMRs are similar to those of LRs \[80\]. Figure 9 shows a graph
comparing the results from these different authors. The [studies are
understandably largely based on projected quantities of materials since
[there are]{.mark} virtually [no SMRs on **commercial**
operations]{.mark} as yet]{.underline}.

Figure 9. LCA Results according to various authors.

As far as greenhouse gas emissions are concerned, studies have been
clearly made with respect to the entire plant in consideration. However,
it will be of equal importance what the environmental effects of such
modularity can have in either implementations i.e., of modularizing the
production process of a single plant, or the modularization of the power
producing capacity. In the former case, one can surmise the increased
cost of transporting the modular parts of a plant to the assembly area.
In the latter case, a nuclear plant with multiple SMRs would possibly
have more structural elements incorporated to secure each reactor.

[With the ever-[increasing **concern** for]{.mark}
environmentally-[sustainable tech]{.mark}nologies, these studies carried
out as accurate as possible are [important for]{.mark} the
decision-making process for [**newcomer** countries to]{.mark} avoid
making that trade-off of the environment for development. It is
important that before a country [make]{.mark}s [a decision to
choose]{.mark} a certain technology path, in this case [SMRs]{.mark},
they **know** the environmental impact it may have even [before
**embarking** on it]{.mark}]{.underline}. This breaks a circular notion
that in order for one to know the environmental impact, we need to build
the plant first, but to choose what plant to build, we need to know what
the environmental effects are. The LCA has to be conducted from the
whole plant-design level as early as possible.

ADVANTAGES OF SMRs

The [**accelerated** pace of R&D into [SMRs]{.mark} has]{.underline}
resulted in [several benefits that are **inherently linked** to the
small and modular design of the systems. SMRs have a **lower** initial
capital **investment**, greater **scalability** and siting
**flexibility** for locations unable to accommodate more traditional
larger reactors. They also have the potential for enhanced **safety**
and **security** compared to earlier designs, and their deployment can
help [**drive** economic **growth**]{.mark}]{.underline}. These benefits
are highlighted below.9

Modularity and Lower Capital Investment

We\'ve already discussed how [the size of SMRs [allows for]{.mark} more
[flexibility]{.mark} in how and where these systems can be
deployed]{.underline}. As the technology continues to advance, [SMRs are
anticipated to require limited on-site preparation, which will
significantly **[reduce]{.mark}** the typical lengthy [**construction**
times]{.mark}]{.underline} associated with larger nuclear units. It is
expected that the ability to transport prefabricated modules to their
required locations will lower project capital costs and ultimately
reduce costs associated with their deployment. As the demand for energy
increases, additional modules can be incrementally added, increasing the
duration of the system\'s life cycle.

Greater Siting Flexibility and Efficiency

[[Ensuring]{.mark} adequate grid [**infrastructure** to]{.mark} deliver
electricity to **[remote locations]{.mark}** remains a significant
challenge in [reducing]{.mark} greenhouse gas (**GHG**) [emissions and
meeting]{.mark} our **energy [security]{.mark}** [needs]{.mark}. SMRs
can provide power in locations where larger traditional units are not
needed or **lack** the **infrastructure** to support a large unit,
including smaller electrical markets, remote areas, smaller grids, sites
with limited water and acreage, or unique industrial applications. SMRs
can also be used for **[replacement]{.mark}** power [at]{.mark} aging or
[retiring]{.mark} fossil-fueled [generation sites]{.mark}. Likewise,
SMRs can [**supplement** existing]{.mark} industrial
**[processes]{.mark}** or generation sites with a carbon-free emitting
energy source]{.underline}. When used with other generation sources,
such as renewable and fossil-fueled energy, [SMRs can **[leverage
resources]{.mark}** and produce higher efficiencies and multiple energy
end products [while increasing **grid resiliency** and
**security**]{.mark}]{.underline}. Some advanced SMRs can produce higher
temperature process heat for either electricity generation or high
energy-intensive industrial applications.

Increased Safeguards

[SMRs include increased [safeguards]{.mark} and security requirements as
part of the "security by design" engineering process applied to new SMR
design]{.underline}. DOE indicates that new SMR design facility
protection systems include "barriers that can withstand design basis
aircraft crash scenarios and other specific threats,"10 and [further
"[provide]{.mark} safety and potential **[nonprolif]{.mark}eration
[benefits]{.mark}** to the United States and the wider international
community."]{.underline}11 DOE further states that "most SMRs will be
built below grade for safety and security enhancements, addressing
vulnerabilities to both sabotage and natural phenomena hazard scenarios.
Some SMRs will be designed to operate for extended periods without
refueling."12 [With the ability to be manufactured and fueled within a
factory, then sealed and transported to a location, advanced SMRs could
be returned to the factory at the end of their life cycle for defueling,
thereby **[minimizing]{.mark}** the transport and handling of **[nuclear
material]{.mark}**]{.underline}. According to DOE, [advanced SMRs "based
on non-light water reactor coolants could be more effective at
dispositioning plutonium while [minimizing]{.mark} the [wastes]{.mark}
requiring disposal."]{.underline}13

Industry Growth and Economic Development

[The [increasing use]{.mark} of any technology [**results** in increased
economic growth]{.mark}. It is anticipated that [**mass manufacturing**
SMRs]{.mark}]{.underline} will reduce the cost per kilowatt of
electricity equal to current generation sources. SMR proponents say that
a factory approach to manufacturing could help the sector cut costs and
remain competitive as the costs of renewables decline. With the
existence of both domestic and international markets for SMRs, DOE is
hopeful that as the development of standardized SMR designs takes hold,
US companies will have an increased presence in the global energy
markets.14 [As with any technology, the increased use of SMRs [**will**
provide]{.mark} the necessary **incentive** to increase manufacturing
capacity to meet demand, resulting in **[further]{.mark}** growth of
both domestic and international sales of SMR units. Coupled with the
deployment of SMR units to replace retiring power plants or meet growing
electricity generation needs, this will result in **[significant
growth]{.mark}** in domestic manufacturing, the tax base, and factory,
construction and operating jobs]{.underline}.

#### That solves [extinction]{.underline}.

**Wong '22** \[Ruth and Eevee Ciara; September 1; Senior Cloud Systems
Engineer, M.A. from Johns Hopkins University, M.Sc. in Bioinformatics
from Western Univrsity; Machine learning engineer at PayPal, writer for
the Effective Altruism Forum; Substack, "Rethinking longtermism and
global development,"
https://sunyshore.substack.com/p/rethinking-longtermism-and-global\]

We agree that more powerful countries are likely to have more influence
on the long-term future than less powerful ones, and that a country's
economic [[development is a]{.mark} **strong [indicator]{.mark}**
[of]{.mark} its **tech**nological and **geopolitical [power]{.mark}**.
However, this]{.underline} does not mean that developed countries matter
morally more than developing ones. Rather, it [underscores the
importance of global development]{.underline}---the process of low- and
middle-income countries becoming high-income ones. Similarly, although
workers in high-income countries generally have higher labor
productivity, a large component of this is the place premium. That is, a
worker's productivity increases---sometimes by a factor of 15 or
more---when they move from a poor country to a rich country, simply
because they are being paid more for the same work. Once again, [this
speaks to the **import**ance of **global development** and helping
people **escape poverty**]{.underline}. This essay describes why it's
important for the long term future for everyone to be in a relatively
high-income country, and what it might look like for global development
to be a longtermist issue. [Global [development benefits]{.mark} the
**long-term [future]{.mark}** by increasing diversity in **global
institutions** and [reducing]{.mark} civilization's [vulnerability
to]{.mark} **global [catastrophic risks]{.mark}** [like]{.mark} **armed
[conflict]{.mark}** [and **pandemics**]{.mark}]{.underline}. What is
global development? Effective altruists regularly talk about "global
health and development" as a category of ways to do good. However, it's
clear now that development drives health much more than health drives
development. Global development is the process by which low- and
middle-income countries turn into high-income countries. The best modern
example of this are the Asian countries of South Korea, Taiwan, Japan,
and China. These have been compared with other Asian countries that were
thought to be "tiger cubs" in the '90s such as the Philippines and
Thailand in the book How Asia Works, by Joe Studwell. In this book, Joe
lays out a recipe for development. Here's the summary from Bill Gates:
Create conditions for small farmers to thrive. Use the proceeds from
agricultural surpluses to build a manufacturing base that is tooled from
the start to produce exports. Nurture both these sectors (small farming
and export-oriented manufacturing) with financial institutions closely
controlled by the government. Importantly, in South Korea, Taiwan,
Japan, and China, these steps were taken by relatively uncorrupted
leaders who made it their life's work to develop their home country.
Other countries unable to develop had leadership who were swayed by the
neoliberal free-market thinking pervasive at the World Bank and IMF at
the time, or who were otherwise too corrupt or incompetent to stay true
in delivering their development strategy. Global development is
important for the long-term future In an EA Forum post, Beckstead
defines three types of benefits that an intervention can have: proximate
benefits, benefits from speeding up development, and trajectory changes.
Global development would have immediate benefits for people alive today:
economic development in low-income countries means that fewer people
would experience poverty, illness, hunger, and violence. Speeding up
development is speeding up the process by which countries become
high-income, so it would ensure that people realize these benefits
sooner. But most of the benefits of global development are through
trajectory changes that affect the long-term future. We argue that
[global development creates significant **long-term benefits** through
this route. Global development can also lead to **trajectory changes**
in the global political environment that would **not** happen if the
development timeline were **slowed down**, since such changes can be
**locked in** over time]{.underline}. Global development increases
diversity in global governance One of the main ways global development
benefits the far future is through its impact on diversity and inclusion
in world institutions. [[As countries get richer]{.mark}, their [people
get]{.mark} **better [educated]{.mark}** [and]{.mark} thus better placed
to [participate in **institutions**]{.mark}]{.underline} with great
decision-making power over the world, such as multinational
corporations, governments, and international organizations. Increasing
the diversity of decision makers in [global institutions
**[improves]{.mark} the quality** of **world [governance]{.mark}**[,
which]{.mark} enables humanity to better **[navigate existential
risks]{.mark}** and other **global challenges**]{.underline}. Diversity
in global institutions improves their efficacy in two ways. First,
socially diverse groups outperform homogeneous groups at decision-making
because they deliberate more carefully and pay more attention to facts.
They also innovate more because diverse group members bring unique
perspectives. Second, it improves value alignment between these
institutions and humanity as a whole. It has been proposed that humanity
should engage in a "long reflection" to decide what is ultimately of
value before making potentially irreversible decisions regarding its
future. For such decisions to reflect the values and needs of all of
humanity, as many people as possible should be able to participate
meaningfully in the global institutions making these decisions.
[Currently, about **689 million** of the 8 billion people worldwide live
in **extreme poverty**, and they **cannot** participate meaningfully in
world governance]{.underline} as long as their basic needs are not met.
2.9 billion people lack Internet access, which is an important
communication channel through which people make their voices heard on
global issues and influence global institutions. Internet adoption is
uneven across social groups: for example, women, people in rural areas,
and people over age 25 are less likely to have Internet access. These
disparities are especially pronounced in the 46 UN-designated Least
Developed Countries (LDCs). Another barrier to diversity in global
governance is the structure of institutions such as the United Nations,
which is not designed to represent the general will of humanity. UN
institutions represent the will of states---especially the five
permanent members of the UN Security Council: the United States, the
United Kingdom, France, Russia, and China. Although a diverse group of
countries have voices in the UN, their citizens do not, especially in
the case of authoritarian states. Global development reduces existential
risks [**Another** important way in which global development improves
the long-term future is by **reducing existential risks**, particularly
risks from [**pandemics** and **political
instability**]{.mark}]{.underline}. 80,000 Hours estimates that the risk
of a biological existential catastrophe in the next 100 years is about 1
in 1000. [[Poverty]{.mark} makes communities more susceptible to
[spread]{.mark}ing infectious **[diseases]{.mark}**]{.underline}. For
example, a study of Monrovia, Liberia, in 2014 found that people living
in slums who caught the Ebola virus spread it to an average of 3.5 times
more people than people living in rich neighborhoods. This is because
these neighborhoods are overcrowded, contaminated, and lacking in
sanitation and health care infrastructure. Also, malnutrition weakens
the immune systems of poor people, thereby making them more vulnerable
to disease. [Raising national [income improves]{.mark} **population
health** and enables countries to **invest more** in **[public]{.mark}
health [infrastructure]{.mark}**, which makes populations more
[**resilient** to]{.mark} potentially **[catastrophic
pandemics]{.mark}**]{.underline}. [**Global [poverty]{.mark}** also
[causes **existential risk** through]{.mark} its negative effects on
**[international security]{.mark}**. Many developing countries,
particularly weak states, are caught in a **vicious cycle** of poverty,
**corruption**, and **political instability**]{.underline}: "Inept,
corrupt, or weak governance fosters poverty; widespread poverty makes
effective, equitable governance more difficult to achieve; and when weak
governments fail to improve their people's lives, their legitimacy
suffers." [[Weak states]{.mark} often [**engender terrorism** and
**crime**]{.mark} because they are unable to maintain law and order in
their territories]{.underline}. Global development in practice There are
two general ways [to increase the living standards]{.underline} of
people around the world. The first is reducing barriers to immigration
to high-income countries so that more people from low-income countries
can move to them, take advantage of the place premium, and send money
back to their countries of origin. The second is to [pursue policies to
increase the productivity of low-income countries so that their people
are better off.]{.underline} Reducing barriers to migration Opening up
borders in high-income countries does not decrease opportunities for
those in high-income countries (although it may stress city
infrastructure if not planned well). Matthew Yglesias writes about a
six-month period in 1980 where 125,000 Cubans left the Cuban port of
Mariel and arrived in Florida (a 7% increase in the labor workforce).
These immigrants weren\'t particularly skilled, but no decreases in
natives' wages occurred. Yglesias explains that immigrants increase the
quality and variety of goods and services available, instead of
competing for native workers' jobs. Furthermore, when the supply of
hired help is increased, some college-educated women who might have
otherwise been stay-at-home moms are more likely to work. Immigrants
also send money back home: for example, according to the Central Bank of
the Philippines, between 1975 and 1994 Filipino overseas contract
workers sent remittances totaling \$18.196 billion (for comparison, the
Philippines is currently \$46B in debt to the World Bank, IMF, etc.).10
Closing the digital divide is also one way to gain some of the benefits
of open borders, by opening up opportunities for jobs in high-income
countries that can be done remotely to people in lower income
countries.11 Catch-up growth [Economic growth improves quality of life
better than public health interventions]{.underline}. However, it is
difficult for a country to increase its income unless the country\'s
leadership is both dedicated to this cause, and following the correct
strategy towards development. As pointed out by a report from Rethink
Priorities last year, land redistribution (the first step of developing
a country according to How Asia Works) is unlikely to happen without
some kind of major turmoil (such as a country gaining independence) as
landowners have political power, even if landowners are vastly
outnumbered. Here are some examples of organizations pursuing catch-up
growth in low- and middle-income countries: The World Bank and IMF
provide loans and advice to low and middle income countries for
development. Depending on the income of the country, the loan may not
have to be repaid. The Gates Foundation provides entrepreneurial
support. Newer organizations working on the problem include Growth Teams
and the Charter Cities Institute. Entrepreneurs such as Sierra Juice and
Jaza Energy contribute by providing jobs and increasing production value
of exports. Interestingly, investment in non-hydro renewable energy has
actually tipped to be greater in low- and middle-income countries than
in high-income countries, now that solar has become cheaper. The Center
for Global Development advocates for rich-world policies that benefit
low- and middle-income countries. Other organizations engage in
activities such as microloans, which are shown to benefit the recipient
as long as the microloan is not used to fund people joining multi-level
marketing schemes. Organizations like Grameen America should stop
allowing the loans to be used for this purpose. Overall there are many
more initiatives meant to alleviate poverty than to encourage the
economic development and democratic power necessary to end it. [Given
that government-led development is hard without a people in power
dedicated to and capable of executing development strategy]{.underline},
**[the mechanism by which [development happens]{.mark} is [by successful
businesses giving]{.mark} people stable [jobs]{.mark}]{.underline}**,
especially jobs that upskill workers in their career. Conclusion While a
naive application of longtermism might lead one to think that low-income
countries are less important than high-income countries, this view is
facile because it ignores the fact that countries can and do develop
over time. Rather, longtermism speaks to the importance of developing
low-income countries into high-income ones. [Global development improves
the long-term future of humanity by improving global governance and

#### Proliferation leads to extinction ☹

Andrew **Richardson**. (\"Nuclear Proliferation to Terrorist Actors and

**[[challenges]{.underline}]{.mark}** in the coming years. [Many
[members]{.mark}]{.underline} of the international community
[[question]{.mark} the [commitment of nuclear weapon]{.mark} states [to

been criticized by the United States as divisive to the broader
nonproliferation regime \[2\]. While the TPNW is a sign of an effort to
speed up the process of abolishing nuclear weapons, rational deterrence
theory suggests that, today's international security landscape, in part,
depends on the presence of nuclear weapons for stability. Well known
nuclear scholars such as Matthew Kroenig posit that, at least for the

vast nuclear capabilities \[3\]. In essence, the [slow steps taken by

[hinders]{.underline} their [ability to limit]{.underline} the
[proliferation of **nuclear weapons**]{.underline}. As this process
continues, it is possible that [more [states **abandon**]{.mark} the

[seek to **acquire WMDs** themselves]{.underline}. Amidst the uncertain

**[emerging]{.mark}**]{.underline}. [[North Korea]{.underline}]{.mark}

#### SMR's are a [tool of diplomatic engagement]{.underline} and [establish US energy leadership]{.underline} abroad. Demand is rising, and SMR's [flexibility in deploymen]{.underline}t and [cost-effectiveness]{.underline} make it the [best solution for clean energy]{.underline}. 

Antonia-Laura **Pup '25**\-\--Teaching Assistant in Security Studies at

(\"Advancing U.S. SMR Diplomacy for a Safe and Secure Energy Transition:

[Small modular reactors [represent a **transformative approach**]{.mark}
to nuclear energy [because]{.mark} [of]{.mark} their [**scalability**
and **flexibility**]{.mark}]{.underline}. The miniaturization of nuclear
reactors is not new. It has been used as early as the 1960s for military
purposes in submarines and aircraft carriers. The contemporary SMR
concept took shape in the early 2000s, with several companies from the
United States, Russia, the People's Republic of China (PRC), Argentina,
Canada, and South Korea pursuing parallel development paths. [One of the
early commercial contributors in the United States, NuScale Power, began
the work on design]{.underline} in the 2000s [and partnered with the
Department of Energy in 2013]{.underline}. NuScale became the first
company to receive approval from the U.S. Nuclear Regulatory Commission
for small modular reactor design in 2020.

[Unlike traditional nuclear power plants built on-site, [SMRs]{.mark}
are [designed in factories]{.mark}]{.underline}. [This]{.underline}
