The [[rising]{.mark} **global [interest]{.mark}** [in SMRs]{.mark} is

[energy challenges]{.mark} simultaneously]{.underline}. Their smaller

Understanding SMR Technology: A New Generation of Nuclear Reactors

Small Modular Reactors, or [[SMRs]{.underline}]{.mark}, [represent a
significant **[depart]{.mark}**ure [from **tradition**]{.mark}al
large-scale nuclear power plants, primarily distinguished by their
reduced size and modular construction]{.underline}. These advanced
nuclear reactors are engineered to produce electrical power outputs
typically ranging from a few megawatts to around 300 megawatts, a stark
contrast to the gigawatt-scale capacities of conventional reactors. This
[[size reduction]{.mark} is not merely a scaling down of existing
technology; it involves innovative design principles that facilitate
factory fabrication, [enabling]{.mark} a more [streamlined and
cost-effective]{.mark} manufacturing [process]{.mark}]{.underline}. The
modular nature of SMRs allows for their construction in controlled
factory environments, followed by transportation to the deployment site,
which [significantly [reduces]{.mark} on-site construction time and
associated **[costs]{.mark}**, a major barrier for traditional nuclear
builds]{.underline}. This approach also [[allows]{.mark} for greater
**flexibility** in deployment locations, making nuclear energy
**[access]{.mark}**ible [to]{.mark} a broader **range** of
[communities]{.mark} and industries]{.underline}. The modular design
also allows for incremental deployment, adding modules as needed to
match growing energy demands, [an approach that is particularly
[attractive for investors]{.mark}]{.underline}. Various SMR designs are
under development, each employing different technologies and offering
unique advantages. Pressurized water reactors (PWRs), a well-established
technology, are adapted for SMR applications, leveraging the existing
knowledge base and infrastructure. Boiling water reactors (BWRs) are
another common design choice, known for their simplicity and efficiency.
However, the field of [SMRs]{.underline} is also pushing the boundaries
of nuclear technology with the development of molten salt reactors
(MSRs), which [offer enhanced safety features and the potential for
greater fuel efficiency]{.underline}. These advanced reactor designs are
exploring innovative cooling mechanisms, fuel cycles, and safety
systems, representing a significant leap forward in nuclear technology.
The [**[diversity]{.mark}** in SMR designs also [allows]{.mark} for
greater flexibility in meeting specific energy needs, from electricity
generation to industrial process heat and
**[desalination]{.mark}**]{.underline}. The innovation in SMR technology
extends beyond reactor design to include advanced materials, digital
control systems, and remote monitoring capabilities, [all contributing
to safer, more efficient, and more reliable operations. These
advancements are crucial for **[addressing]{.mark}** the challenges
associated with nuclear energy, including **[waste]{.mark}** management
[and **prolif**]{.mark}**eration** [concerns]{.mark}]{.underline}.
Furthermore, the [development and deployment of SMRs are not just about
technological innovation; they also have significant implications for

sustainable energy. The potential of [SMRs]{.mark} to [provide clean,
reliable, and affordable energy]{.mark} is [attracting]{.mark}
**significant [interest]{.mark}**]{.underline} from both public and

solution, offering a pathway to a more sustainable and resilient energy
future. The [**regulatory** frameworks]{.mark} and policies surrounding
SMRs [are **evolving**]{.mark} to accommodate the unique characteristics

[deployment]{.mark}]{.underline}. The development of standardized

key component of the future of energy.

\[TEXT CONDENSED, NONE OMMITTED\]

Advantages of SMRs: Safety, Affordability, and Accessibility Small
Modular Reactors (SMRs) present a paradigm shift in nuclear energy,
offering a range of advantages over traditional large-scale nuclear
power plants. Their inherent safety features, often based on passive
systems, minimize the risk of accidents by relying on natural forces
like gravity and convection for cooling, rather than complex active
systems. This enhanced safety profile, coupled with smaller size and
modularity, significantly reduces capital costs and construction
timelines, attracting investment and accelerating deployment. For
instance, NuScale Power's SMR design leverages natural circulation for
cooling, eliminating the need for large pumps and reducing the
probability of a core meltdown. This modular approach also allows for
factory fabrication and transportation, streamlining on-site
construction and minimizing project risks, ultimately contributing to a
more predictable and cost-effective energy solution. SMRs are
particularly well-suited for remote locations and smaller grids,
expanding access to clean and reliable energy for communities previously
reliant on fossil fuels. Their flexible deployment options make them
ideal for powering remote mines, military bases, or isolated
communities, promoting economic development and energy independence.
Furthermore, SMRs offer the potential for non-electric applications,
such as desalination, district heating, and process heat generation for
industrial processes, further diversifying their utility and
contributing to sustainable resource management. This versatility
positions SMRs as a key technology in addressing climate change by
providing a carbon-free energy source for a wide range of applications.
The lower upfront capital costs associated with SMRs also reduce the
financial barriers to entry for smaller utilities and developing
nations, promoting wider access to nuclear energy and supporting global
decarbonization efforts. From an investment perspective, SMRs represent
a compelling opportunity, with their modular design reducing
construction risks and offering the potential for faster return on
investment. Government policies and regulations play a crucial role in
shaping SMR deployment, with streamlined licensing processes and
supportive regulatory frameworks being essential for accelerating the
adoption of this transformative technology. The development and
deployment of SMRs also stimulate innovation in related fields, such as
advanced materials, manufacturing techniques, and digital control
systems, creating new economic opportunities and driving technological
advancements. As the world transitions to a cleaner energy future, SMRs
hold the potential to be a key player in achieving global climate goals,
enhancing energy security, and fostering sustainable economic growth.
The reduced land footprint required for SMRs compared to conventional
nuclear plants minimizes environmental impact and enhances public
acceptance, making them a more palatable option for communities
concerned about land use and visual impact. International collaboration
is essential for accelerating SMR development and deployment, sharing
best practices, and addressing common challenges related to safety,
security, and waste management. These partnerships are fostering a
global SMR ecosystem, driving innovation and ensuring the responsible
and sustainable advancement of this promising technology. Addressing the
Challenges of SMR Deployment While Small Modular Reactors (SMRs) hold
immense potential to revolutionize nuclear energy, their deployment is
not without challenges. Navigating the regulatory landscape presents a
significant hurdle. The licensing processes for SMRs, while anticipated
to be more streamlined than those for traditional large-scale reactors,
still involve complex procedures and stringent safety evaluations by
organizations like the Nuclear Regulatory Commission (NRC) in the United
States. This can lead to extended timelines and increased development
costs, potentially hindering the pace of SMR deployment. International
harmonization of regulatory frameworks could expedite the licensing
process and facilitate global SMR adoption. Public perception and
acceptance remain paramount for the successful integration of SMRs.
Addressing public concerns regarding nuclear safety, waste disposal, and
potential environmental impacts requires transparent communication,
community engagement, and accessible educational resources. Building
trust and fostering open dialogue with stakeholders are essential for
gaining public support and ensuring the social license to operate.
Effective waste management strategies are crucial for the long-term
sustainability of SMRs. Although SMRs generate less waste than
conventional reactors, the development of robust and secure waste
disposal solutions, including advanced recycling technologies and
geological repositories, is vital. International cooperation and the
sharing of best practices in waste management can further enhance the
safety and efficiency of these processes. Concerns about nuclear
proliferation and the potential misuse of SMR technology require careful
consideration. Implementing robust safeguards and security measures,
along with international agreements and monitoring mechanisms, are
essential to mitigate these risks. Transparency and collaboration among
nations are crucial for ensuring the peaceful and responsible
development of SMR technology. Integrating SMRs into existing power
grids presents technical complexities. Their smaller scale and modular
nature require adjustments to grid infrastructure and control systems to
ensure stable and reliable electricity supply. Investing in grid
modernization and developing advanced grid management technologies are
essential for seamless SMR integration and maximizing their potential
benefits. The economic viability of SMRs is a key factor influencing
their widespread adoption. While SMRs offer potential cost advantages
through modular construction and factory fabrication, achieving
competitive electricity prices requires careful cost management,
efficient supply chains, and supportive government policies. Investment
in research and development, along with public-private partnerships, can
drive down costs and accelerate the commercialization of SMR technology.
The future of SMRs depends on addressing these challenges effectively.
By fostering innovation, streamlining regulations, engaging with the
public, and developing robust safety and security protocols, the
transformative potential of SMRs can be unlocked, paving the way for a
cleaner, more sustainable, and secure energy future. The Global SMR
Landscape: A Race to Innovation The global landscape of Small Modular
Reactor (SMR) development is rapidly evolving, marked by substantial
investments and collaborative efforts across several nations. The United
States, for instance, is actively pursuing SMR technology through
initiatives like the Department of Energy's Advanced Reactor
Demonstration Program, which aims to accelerate the commercialization of
innovative designs. This includes support for companies developing both
light-water and non-light-water SMRs, reflecting a strategic push
towards a diverse nuclear energy portfolio. Similarly, Canada's
commitment to SMRs is evident through its national SMR roadmap,
emphasizing the technology's potential for both on-grid and off-grid
applications, particularly in remote communities and industrial sites.
This initiative is not just about energy generation; it's also about
fostering regional economic growth and technological leadership. China,
with its ambitious nuclear power expansion plans, is also heavily
invested in SMRs, viewing them as a key component of its energy strategy
to meet rising demands while reducing carbon emissions, highlighting the
crucial role of SMRs in addressing climate change. Russia, a
long-standing player in the nuclear sector, continues to advance its SMR
designs, leveraging its existing nuclear infrastructure and expertise to
maintain its position in the global nuclear market. These diverse
approaches highlight a global race for innovation in the SMR space,
driven by the need for reliable, clean, and secure energy sources. These
are clear signals of the potential SMRs hold for the future of energy.
The development of Small Modular Reactors is not confined to
government-led initiatives; numerous private companies worldwide are
contributing to the innovation ecosystem. Companies are actively
developing diverse SMR designs, ranging from advanced light water
reactors to molten salt reactors and high-temperature gas-cooled
reactors. These designs offer varying characteristics in terms of fuel
type, safety features, and operational parameters, reflecting a broad
spectrum of technological approaches to SMR development. International
collaborations are becoming increasingly important, with joint ventures
and partnerships forming to share knowledge, resources, and expertise.
Such collaborations are crucial for accelerating the pace of innovation
and facilitating the commercialization of SMR technology. These joint
efforts also play a key role in standardizing regulatory frameworks and
addressing concerns related to nuclear safety and security. The
investment landscape for SMRs is also dynamic, with venture capital,
private equity, and government funding playing crucial roles in
financing research, development, and deployment projects. Investors are
increasingly recognizing the potential of SMRs to address the global
energy transition while generating attractive returns, driving further
financial interest in the sector. The regulatory landscape for SMRs is
also evolving, with agencies in various countries working to establish
clear and efficient licensing processes. These regulatory efforts are
essential for ensuring the safety and security of SMR operations while
also providing a predictable pathway for commercial deployment. Policy
makers are also playing a key role, implementing measures to incentivize
SMR development and deployment, recognizing their role in addressing
climate change and achieving energy security. These efforts are paving
the way for the wider adoption of SMR technology as a key component of a
sustainable energy future. This diverse array of activities demonstrates
that the future of energy will be significantly influenced by SMRs.

The Future of SMRs: A Path to Sustainable Nuclear Energy

The future of Small Modular Reactors ([SMRs]{.underline}) hinges on a
confluence of factors, including continuous technological advancements,
robust regulatory frameworks, and strong public-private partnerships.
These advanced reactors [represent a potential paradigm shift in energy
production, offering a sustainable and]{.underline} potentially
[**game-changing [solution]{.mark}** [to climate change]{.mark}. To

1\. Introduction

[In recent years, there has been [**widespread interest** in
SMRs]{.mark}]{.underline} (small modular reactors). These would generate
between 10 and 300 MWe of electricity, with power levels much smaller
than those of reactor designs that are now standard \[1e3\]. [These
reactors are aimed at solving some of the multiple problems plaguing the
nuclear industry and allow the possibility of using nuclear power
[in]{.mark} **market niches** that have previously been difficult to
enter. These market niches include **[developing countries]{.mark}**
with smaller electric grids, **[remote locations]{.mark}**, water
**desalination**]{.underline}, and industrial heat supply \[4e6\].

There are a very wide variety of SMR designs with distinct
characteristics that are being developed. Many of them are light water
reactors, the leading reactor-type that is currently deployed around the
world,1 but there are also many designs that are radically different
(Table 1). Several countries are developing and planning to construct
SMRs, including the United States, Russia, China, France, Japan, South
Korea, India, and Argentina. [Many [expect the SMR **market** to be
**large**]{.mark}. One widely cited assessment]{.underline} from 2009
[concludes that there could be between 43 and 96 small modular reactors
(SMRs) in operation around the world by 2030]{.underline} \[8\]. In
2006, Advanced Systems Technology and Management, Inc., a management
consulting company, projected that [SMRs will capture [**30 percent** of
the future market]{.mark} for nuclear reactors]{.underline}, which it
estimated at around 1000 GW; assuming "an average SMR size of 300 MWe,
this implies some 1000 units by 2050" \[9\]. For the United States, the
Energy Policy Institute in Idaho projected a "moderate case" involving
about 150 SMRs and a "disruptive case" involving about 550 units by 2030
\[10\]. [Though these figures are only tentative projections, the
[belief]{.mark} that there will be a substantial market for SMRs seems
to be widely held [by]{.mark} policy makers in **[many
countries]{.mark}**]{.underline}. There are, of course, many who are
skeptical of this proposition \[12,13\].

Further, [the general expectation is that the [**first movers** will
have]{.mark} a considerable [advantage in]{.mark} capturing the
[global]{.mark} SMR [market]{.mark} and this would help create domestic
jobs in the design and manufacture of these technologies]{.underline}.
Partly for this reason, many countries have provided substantial
government support for the development of such reactors. In the United
States, for example, the Department of Energy has offered up to \$452
million to support engineering, design certification, and licensing of
two SMR designs. However, most of these countries also realize that
there are multiple vendors offering such products. [The
U.S.]{.underline} Department of Commerce's International Trade
Administration, for example, [sees]{.underline} "[**intense** foreign
**competition**]{.underline}, primarily by state-owned or statealigned
enterprises" [as a significant challenge or obstacle]{.underline}
\[14\]. Likewise, South Korea also stresses "export competitiveness"
because of "intense competition among nuclear suppliers" \[15\].

There are multiple challenges that confront vendors of SMRs \[16\], with
an important one being their licensing. While not strictly necessary for
exports to other countries, [the general expectation is that each SMR
design should be [licensed in]{.mark} one or more countries, typically
their [**"home" country** as the **first step** to
deployment]{.mark}]{.underline}.4 This, in turn, is based on the
assumption that [[countries]{.mark} may be [**more hesitant** to
purchase]{.mark} a [SMR when the design has not received its
**originating** nation's]{.mark} regulatory stamp of
**[approval]{.mark}**]{.underline}.5 The IAEA (International Atomic
Energy Agency) offers the following recommendation to countries
considering their first nuclear power plant: "[Choosing a nuclear
reactor design that is [finalized]{.mark} and frozen, particularly one
that has undergone licensing [review]{.mark} in other countries, can
**[minimize]{.mark}** project **[uncertainties]{.mark}**. While some
modifications may be needed due to local regulatory requirements or due
to the special characteristics of a site, a complete design helps to
ensure that the project will be **within budget** and
**schedule**]{.underline}" \[18\].

**Aumeier '23** \[Steven, David Shropshir, Kathleen Araújo; Idaho

[[Eielson]{.mark} AF [Base]{.mark} was [named]{.mark} as a military
installation [to]{.mark} site and **[demonstrate]{.mark} the
[feasibility]{.mark}** [of a]{.mark} [microreactor]{.mark}]{.underline}.
This is being done to demonstrate the capability of an MR to supply
power in the event that the base's main source of power goes offline
(Ellis, 2022). Currently, Eielson AF Base uses a 70-year-old, 15 MW
coal-fired heat and power plant, with diesel fuel for back-up (Ibid).
The request for proposals reflects a call for construction of a facility
to [accommodate an MR that would generate up to 5 MW and operate for 10
years, until its fuel is spent]{.underline}. "The plan calls for
construction to begin in three years and for the reactor to begin
generating power in 2027" (Ibid). The MR will be licensed by the U.S.
Nuclear Regulatory Commission, and commercially owned and operated. This
initiative reflects actions for the Microreactor Pilot Program that were
initiated in response to the Fiscal Year 2019 National Defense
Authorization Act requirement to construct and operate an MR by the end
of 2027 (Sec. of the Air Force, 2022).

Alaska - Remote Communities, Studies, and Legislation

[[Alaska has **unique energy needs**]{.mark}, with remote communities
reflecting considerably]{.underline} [higher electricity
costs]{.underline}. Compared to the December 2022 national average power
cost of 15 cents/kWh, the weighted average residential rate before the
Power Cost Equalization (PCE) [Program support was paid in Alaska
equaled]{.underline} 47 cents/kWh (EIA, 2023; AEA, 2023) (see also Nome
Energy Overview for more discussion of PCE costs). [These
[challenges]{.mark} have [spurred]{.mark} **communities** and **[energy
cooperatives]{.mark}** in the region [to look for]{.mark} more
[**cost-effective** solutions]{.mark}]{.underline}. In the early 2000s,
the remote village of Galena studied the potential for installing a
small nuclear reactor with Toshiba corporation, but the technology and
regulation were not ready (Interviews, 2022-2023; Chaney et al. 2008).
Since then, many new SMRs have been developed and are being examined as
options across the U.S. and abroad. Copper Valley Electric Association
near Glennallen, Alaska is exploring the possible deployment of a MR.
The University of Alaska Fairbanks has been watching the development of
the technology, especially availability to permit and deploy (Rhode,
2022). Copper Valley Electric has partnered with Ultra Safe Nuclear
Corporation to assess the technical feasibility of a 10MW microreactor
facility, considering cost, operations and social acceptance regionally
(Ultra Safe Nuclear, 2022). In May 2022, [Alaska also passed significant
legislation through SB 177 which designated the Department of
Environmental Conservation to permit nuclear reactors rather than the
Legislature, in the [hopes of helping]{.mark} Alaskan
[communities]{.mark} to **[reach]{.mark} [net-zero carbon]{.mark}
goals**]{.underline} (Segall, 2022). (See also Policies Section).

Adapting the legal and regulatory framework to accelerate the
development and deployment of nuclear technologies and increase the use
of nuclear power in the energy mix has become a national priority in the
United States.

Strengthening and Financing the Nuclear Fuel Cycle

As nuclear has made a dramatic comeback these last few years after
decades of disinterest, the nuclear sector needs to rebuild not only its
supply chain but also its workforce. These last few years have seen a
resurgence of innovation through the development of numerous new
reactors by historic nuclear vendors (i.e. Westinghouse and GE-Hitachi),
national laboratories (i.e. Idaho and Oak Ridge) and start-ups (i.e.
Holtec, NuScale, Last Energy and X-Energy). According to the
International Atomic Energy Agency (\"IAEA\"), over 20 small modular
reactor (\"SMR\") designs are currently under development in the U.S.,
and around 15 SMR start-ups have been established---highlighting the
vibrant innovation driving the American nuclear sector.

The \"[[**Reinvigorating** the Nuclear Industrial Base]{.mark}\"
Executive Order]{.underline} includes provisions that [[strengthen
**education**]{.mark}**al** pathways [in the industry and
increases]{.mark}]{.underline} potential [[funding of nuclear
projects]{.mark}.]{.underline} The Executive Order [directs the
Secretary of Energy]{.underline}, through the Loan Programs Office of
the Department of Energy (\"DOE\"), [to prioritize making available
resources]{.underline} for restarting closed nuclear power plants,
increase power output of operating nuclear power plants, complete
construction of nuclear reactors that were prematurely suspended,
construct new advanced SMRs, and improve the nuclear fuel supply chain.
By the end of this year, the Secretary of Energy and the Administrator
of the Small Business Administration must prioritize funding for
qualified advanced nuclear reactor technologies through grants, loans,
investment capital, funding opportunities, and other available support.
In this respect, the DOE is also instructed to prioritize work with the
nuclear energy industry to facilitate 5 gigawatt of power uprates to
existing nuclear reactors and have 10 new large reactors with complete
designs under construction by 2030. [[One of the **latest

the DOE\'s Generation III+ SMR program^2^, which [is [intended to fund

#### They possess economic and technical advantages enabling deployment.

**Iyer '14** \[Gokul, Nathan Hultman, Steve Fetter, Son Kim; July 6;
Earth Scientist at JGCRI and University of Maryland; Energy Economics,
"Implications of small modular reactors for climate change mitigation,"
vol. 45\]

From an [**economic** standpoint]{.underline}, the smaller size of the
**[[SMR]{.underline}]{.mark}** means a potential loss of economies of
scale in generation associated with large reactors, but [[**promises**
future economies]{.underline}]{.mark} of scale [[in **manufacture** and
**deployment**]{.underline}]{.mark}. Recent expert elicitations have
reported higher overnight (capital) costs for SMRs compared to GW-scale
Gen II and Gen III systems (Abdulla et al., 2013; Anadon et al., 2012;
Anadón et al., 2013). Nevertheless, [[SMRs have]{.underline}]{.mark} a
[**number** of [technical and economic
**advantages**]{.mark}]{.underline} compared to large reactors (Carelli
et al., 2010; Ingersoll, 2009; Kessides and Kuznetsov, 2012; Kuznetsov,
2008; Rosner and Goldberg, 2011). First, unlike large reactors, [SMR
[designs are]{.mark} **[compact]{.mark}**]{.underline} because a number
of [components such as steam gen]{.underline}erators,
[pressurizer]{.underline} and reactor coolant pumps are integrated
within the reactor vessel itself rather than outside of the reactor.
Most [SMR designs [incorporate]{.mark} passive
**[safety]{.mark}**]{.underline} features2 [[that **reduce**]{.mark} or
**eliminate**]{.underline} the [[risk of fuel damage]{.mark} and
radiation]{.underline} releases related to loss of coolant or loss of
coolant flow. In addition, other features of the SMR such as a larger
surface-to-volume ratio and reduced core power density, facilitate
easier removal of heat and the use of advanced passive features (Bae et
al., 2001; Carelli et al., 2004; IAEA, 2009). SMRs also [have a [smaller
fuel inventory]{.mark}]{.underline} which [[reduces]{.mark} the
[maximum]{.mark}]{.underline} possible [[release during]{.mark} an
**[adverse event]{.mark}**]{.underline} (Kessides, 2012).

Second, because of their smaller sizes, [SMRs]{.underline} would
[[require reduced **construction** times]{.underline}]{.mark} and
[[therefore **smaller**]{.mark} interest
**[payments]{.mark}**]{.underline} during [construction]{.underline}
(Abdulla et al., 2013). In other words, [SMRs]{.underline} are [[likely
to be financially **less risky**]{.underline}]{.mark} compared to large
reactors. Third, [the [modularity]{.mark}]{.underline} of SMRs [[permits
**scaling**]{.underline}]{.mark} the [[power plant]{.underline}]{.mark}
to larger sizes [[based on]{.mark} [**incremental** needs]{.mark} for
energy]{.underline} and [compatibility]{.underline} with the [electrical
grid infrastructure]{.underline}. Modularity offers other benefits not
only by [[reducing]{.underline}]{.mark} the [[**front-end** investment
and]{.mark} [facilitating]{.mark}]{.underline} initial
[[deployment]{.underline}]{.mark} but also [**enhancing** temporal and
spatial **flexibility**]{.underline} in investment. The latter feature
is an important distinction from large reactors because it creates an
option value: under uncertainty in future electricity prices, investment
in large reactors is very risky as a large portion of the investment is
sunk and irreversible. On the other hand, in spite of higher overnight
costs, the modularity feature of [SMRs [offers]{.mark}]{.underline} a
[[**better control** over market risk]{.mark} to
**investors**]{.underline} (as investment can be split more easily to
match market demand) and so the risk premium is lower(Gollier et al.,
2005).

Finally, [SMRs [can be **mass produced** in]{.mark}]{.underline} a
[[factory]{.underline}]{.mark} and shipped to the site. [Mass
[production could facilitate]{.mark}]{.underline} and [accelerate
**[cost reductions]{.mark}**]{.underline} due to learning. Empirical
evidence on cost reductions due to increasing capacity in the nuclear
industry is mixed. In the past, several scholars found evidence of
learning and experience spillovers leading to a lowering of costs in the
nuclear industry (Lester and McCabe, 1993; Zimmerman, 1982). On the
other hand, other scholars argued that increased construction times due
to increased size and complexity of reactors coupled with new
environmental, health and safety regulations led to escalating capital
as well as operating and maintenance costs (Cantor and Hewlett, 1988;
Hewlett, 1996; Joskow and Rose, 1985). Similar findings have been
reported by more recent studies that emphasize that the site-specific
nature of deployment makes standardization difficult, so cost reductions
have not been achieved and are not likely in the future (Cooper, 2010;
Grübler, 2010; Hultman and Koomey, 2007; Hultman et al., 2007). However,
in the case of SMRs, several scholars argue that cost savings can be
achieved through off-site fabrication of modules (which facilitates
standardization), as well as learning-by-doing through the production of
multiple, simple modules with shorter construction times (Abdulla et
al., 2013; Kessides, 2012; Rosner and Goldberg, 2011). In addition,
Rangel and Lévêque (2012) used detailed data for French reactors and
argued that while overall experience did not translate into lower costs,
some [[gains]{.mark} were [achieved due to]{.mark}]{.underline} the
[[**construction** of]{.mark} standard]{.underline} [[reactor
types]{.underline}]{.mark}. This finding is relevant to SMRs, which are
likely to be co-sited and the same type of reactors are likely to be
produced in larger numbers (Abdulla et al., 2013; Carelli et al., 2010).
In the subsequent section, we discuss some of the policy rationales put
forth by SMR proponents for promoting the deployment of SMRs.

### Solvency\-\--AT: Costs\-\--2AC

#### SMRs are economically viable, reduced manufacturing costs rely on aff deployment. (others studies are wrong bc they only consider factors in isolation) 

**Vanatta et al 24** -- William Stewart, Michael Craig, and Max Vanatta,
University of Michigan School for Environment and Sustainability, Ann
Arbor Doctorate in Resource Policy and Behavior advised by Dr. Michael
Craig. Research centered on a) the energy transition, combining policy,
technology, and equity lenses to understand the future of the power
system and b) economically decarbonization of high heat industrial
industries. "The role of policy and module manufacturing learning in
industrial decarbonization by small modular reactors," Nature Energy
Journal, IS10, pg. 77--89.//ATarnas

https://www.nature.com/articles/s41560-024-01665-w

Thus, [the [decarbonization]{.mark} of industrial heat [with SMRs will
hinge on]{.mark} two factors: [the techno-economic potential of
SMR]{.mark}s in decarbonizing industrial heat processes, [and]{.mark}
the [ability]{.mark} of SMRs [to realize capital cost reductions with
increasing deployment.]{.mark}]{.underline} []{.mark}By jointly
considering these factors, [[SMR deployment pathways]{.mark} in industry
[can be established]{.mark}, [in which economically viable SMR
deployments]{.mark} beget cost reductions that [make other deployments
economically viable]{.mark}.]{.underline} Yet, [[existing studies only
address these factors in isolation]{.mark}.]{.underline} Initial
technological evaluation studies have found that SMRs of varying designs
do have potential in industrial heat applications without consideration
of
economics[4](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41560-024-01665-w#ref-CR4)^,^[20](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41560-024-01665-w#ref-CR20).
Further studies address the techno-economic potential of SMRs assuming
fixed capital
costs[3](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41560-024-01665-w#ref-CR3)^,^[27](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41560-024-01665-w#ref-CR27).
These studies find that, [when in competition with other fuels such as
natural gas or other low-carbon fuels, SMRs can offer the most
profitable pathway]{.underline} when delivered natural gas is at or
above US\$8 per metric million British thermal unit (MMBtu). Other
studies evaluate possible learning rates for SMRs either to quantify the
necessary deployment volume required to reach parity with other
technologies[28](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41560-024-01665-w#ref-CR28) or
to isolate the potential differences with NPPs benefitting from their
scale and
design[23](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41560-024-01665-w#ref-CR23).
Neither of these studies considers the techno-economic potential of SMRs
with specific consumer contexts, which is ultimately what will drive SMR
deployment and learning.

Here, we integrate these factors to achieve two goals: (1) to identify
techno-economically viable deployment pathways for SMRs in industrial
process heat decarbonization and (2) to quantitatively examine the
effect of various policies in driving long-term SMR deployment. [We find
that [there is]{.mark} modest to [substantial potential for SMR]{.mark}
deployment in providing industrial process heat,]{.underline}
[but]{.mark} [[deployment]{.mark} [varies]{.mark} [greatly
with]{.mark}]{.underline} natural gas price, [[policy
incentives]{.mark}, first-of-a-kind (FOAK) cost escalations and ability
to learn within factories.]{.underline} From 2021 US industrial average
natural gas prices of US\$6 per MMBtu to liquified natural gas benchmark

be profitably deployed if projected FOAK costs are met. Of this
deployment, 2--24 GW~t~ rely on module manufacturing learning within a
factory, although FOAK cost escalations associated with pioneer plants
could substantially reduce or eliminate our estimated SMR deployment
potential.

#### Arctic competition heating up now\-\--new energy infrastructure is key.

Heather A. **Conley 25**, Nonresident Senior Fellow, American Enterprise
Institute, Former President, German Marshall Fund, Former Deputy
Assistant Secretary Of State For Europe And European Affairs; \"The
Arctic Great Game,\" Foreign Affairs, 6-24-2025,
https://www.foreignaffairs.com/reviews/arctic-great-game-conley. //TL

With nineteenth-century power dynamics resurgent, the former U.S.
diplomat Mary Thompson-Jones's recent book, America in the Arctic,
offers a timely and informative narrative of how the United States
acquired and maintained its status as an Arctic power. After a largely
successful history of building a U.S. presence in the Arctic,
Thompson-Jones warns, [[Washington]{.underline}]{.mark} is now [[paying
**insufficient**]{.mark} [**attention** to a]{.mark} region that has
become]{.underline} a [[**focus** of the world]{.mark}'s great
powers]{.underline}.

Even in the short time since America in the Arctic was written, [new
developments]{.underline} have [raised the stakes]{.underline}. After
taking office, Trump trained his sights on potential Arctic
acquisitions, making frequent, controversial references to Canada as
"the 51st state" and vowing that the United States would "get"
Greenland, an autonomous territory of Denmark, "one way or another."
[[Cooperation]{.mark} between Russia and China]{.underline}, meanwhile,
has been [[growing since]{.mark} their]{.underline} 2022 [[announcement
of]{.mark} an "**u[nlimited partnership]{.mark}**]{.underline},"
[which]{.underline} in the Arctic has [translated to
**[joint]{.mark}**]{.underline} scientific, space, and [**[military
op]{.mark}eration[s]{.mark}**,]{.underline} including coast guard and
naval patrols. And Washington's recent outreach to Moscow has introduced
a wildcard: should talks yield some kind of grand bargain, the
[resulting **[geopolitical realignment]{.mark}**]{.underline} could
[[change the game]{.underline}]{.mark} entirely.

Whatever happens, a [[**contest** over]{.underline}]{.mark} critical
**[[minerals]{.underline}]{.mark}**, maritime
**[[routes]{.underline}]{.mark}**, fisheries, [natural
**[resources]{.mark}**,]{.underline} seabed [**[mining]{.mark}**,
[and]{.mark}]{.underline} satellite
**[[comm]{.underline}]{.mark}**unication[**[s]{.mark}** is
[coming]{.mark}]{.underline}, and the [U]{.underline}nited
[S]{.underline}tates [is not ready]{.underline} for it. For years,
[Russia and China]{.underline} have been [preparing to take advantage
of]{.underline} new Arctic shipping [routes]{.underline},
**[improving]{.underline}** their undersea **[military]{.underline}**
and scientific **[capabilities]{.underline}**, and [honing]{.underline}
their [hybrid warfare tactics]{.underline} while U.S. attention has been
elsewhere. To compete, the [[U]{.underline}]{.mark}nited
[[S]{.underline}]{.mark}tates [will its]{.underline} [[need to
**dramatically increase**]{.mark}]{.underline} military, economic,
scientific, and diplomatic **[[presence]{.underline}]{.mark}** in the
Arctic, in close cooperation with U.S. allies. If Washington does not
resolve the deficiencies and contradictions of its Arctic strategy soon,
it may find that it has already lost the new great game.

MEET THE CONTESTANTS

Thompson-Jones provides a rich history of the United States' experience
in the Arctic, including its active role in shaping the Arctic policies
of Canada, Denmark (via Greenland), Finland, Iceland, Norway, Russia,
and Sweden, incorporating memorable vignettes from each Arctic country.
A former U.S. diplomat who served in Canada, Thompson-Jones conveys her
deep admiration for the people who live in the Arctic and her
appreciation of the unrelenting effects of climate change, the desire
for security, and the value of friends and allies "when the ice breaks,"
as the Inuit proverb goes. The book closes with a stark---and
accurate---lament of Washington's distinct lack of ambition in its
recent Arctic policies. Thompson-Jones, writing before the U.S.
presidential election last year, recommends that future leaders increase
their focus on climate change and multilateral diplomacy in an expansive
Arctic strategy. That advice, unfortunately, quickly became outdated
with the return of Trump.

More likely to suit the sensibilities of the U.S. president is
Thompson-­Jones's suggestion that the United States have what she calls a
"Longyear moment"---a reference to a Midwestern industrialist named John
Longyear, who in 1901 sailed to the Svalbard archipelago in the sea
north of mainland Norway and "saw iron ore and big possibilities." In
1906, Longyear founded the Arctic Coal Company and sought to build and
sustain an industrial presence in the Arctic, with the eventual support
of the U.S. government. Thompson-Jones writes that this venture
represented a "profound conceptual shift" in U.S. approaches to the
Arctic, ushering in an era of heightened ambition.

Over a century later, the [U]{.underline}nited [S]{.underline}tates
[needs to pursue "big possibilities]{.underline}" in the Arctic once
again if it is [to compete with]{.underline} its rivals, [Russia and
China. All]{.underline} three players [are invested in the
region]{.underline}, but in different ways. For Russia, which holds vast
swaths of Arctic territory, the region is vital to its military and
economic survival. For China, the Arctic represents an opportunity to
diversify its global economic interests. And [for the
U]{.underline}nited [S]{.underline}tates, which secured its Arctic
presence with the 1867 purchase of the territory of Alaska from
Russia---a sale that Dmitry Rogozin, Russia's former deputy prime
minister, has described as a "betrayal of Russian power status"---the
[region is a]{.underline} northern [frontline of defense]{.underline}.

The Arctic animates Russian President Vladimir Putin's geopolitical
strategy. He seeks to develop a maritime passageway, the Northern Sea
Route, that traverses Russia's northern coastal waters and is dotted
with new port infrastructure linked by rail to the country's sub-Arctic
regions. A new fleet of Russian icebreakers would escort registered
vessels along the route, which would facilitate the export of Russian
natural resources and the east-west transit of Chinese goods. In that
kind of large-scale project, Thompson-Jones traces echoes of a brutal
legacy: the savagery of the Soviet leader Joseph Stalin's Arctic
infrastructure campaign, in which roads, railways, and mines were built
by prisoners and forced laborers, many of whom died during the
construction. One road was known as "the Bone Road" because so many
workers were buried in its foundation that "there is one body for every
meter of road."

Putin's economic and military buildup in the region is less ruthless
than Stalin's but similarly ambitious, driven by Russia's chronic sense
of insecurity and fear of losing control over its territory. After the
collapse of the Soviet Union, Arctic military bases were closed, damaged
infrastructure was left unrepaired, and many Arctic populations, cut off
from state subsidies, moved elsewhere. Today, Russian authorities are
trying to prevent a further deterioration of the Arctic population by
delaying residents' requests to leave. Polar gulags are also the
preferred place to send political prisoners who threaten the government,
such as the opposition leader Alexei Navalny, who died under suspicious
circumstances in one such prison in 2024. Russia is constructing and
refurbishing Arctic military bases, in part to improve its monitoring
capabilities as commercial activity increases along the Northern Sea
Route. The sudden appearance of Russian flags, crosses, and Orthodox
priests across not just the Russian Arctic but also, worryingly, the
Norwegian High North are declarations of Russia's past, present, and
future ownership.

\[FIGURE OMITTED\]

China joined the Arctic game more recently. Despite lacking Arctic
territory of its own, China has declared itself a "near Arctic" state on
the basis of fifteenth-century maps and its interest in Arctic
governance. Beginning in 2004, when it established its first research
station on Svalbard, it has used scientific cooperation to boost its
Arctic presence and knowledge. Later, China pursued business ventures
with Canada and the Nordic states, but these countries were wary of its
investment terms---and under pressure from Washington---and slowly
restricted Beijing's access. Another opening came with Russia's invasion
of Ukraine in February 2022. As Moscow faced the loss of its European
markets, the end of its partnerships with Western energy companies, and
wartime budgetary limitations, it welcomed Chinese investment as a way
to fill the gap. China increased funding for Russia's liquefied natural
gas projects in the Arctic and related infrastructure development along
the Northern Sea Route, expanding its commercial presence in the region.

For its part, the United States has been an Arctic economic power since
it acquired Alaska to secure access to the territory's natural
resources. It first attempted to purchase Greenland in 1868 for the same
reason. (Further attempts to acquire the island---in 1910, 1946, and
2019---had a mix of economic and security motives.) After World War II,
the United States expanded its Arctic presence through a network of
regional alliances and infrastructure projects. In the 1950s, it built
the Distant Early Warning Line, a string of radar stations that
traversed Alaska, Canada, Greenland, Iceland, and the Faroe Islands and
remained operational until 1993 to defend against a potential Soviet
missile attack. In cooperation with Canada, the United States
constructed the Alaska Highway and created an integrated air defense
system known as NORAD. Together with NATO allies, U.S. forces patrolled
the waters and airspace of the North Atlantic, particularly around
Greenland, Iceland, and the United Kingdom, to detect Soviet, and later,
Russian nuclear submarines and bombers.

The [[Arctic]{.mark} remains [**vital** to U.S]{.mark}. **economic** and
**security [interests]{.mark}**]{.underline}. Anchorage,
[[Alaska]{.underline}]{.mark}, is [[home to the]{.underline}]{.mark}
[**fourth-[busiest]{.mark}** cargo **[airport]{.mark}**]{.underline} in
the world. Nearly [[all]{.mark} of the **[U]{.mark}**]{.underline}nited
**[[S]{.underline}]{.mark}**tates' **[[radar]{.underline}]{.mark}**
systems **[[and]{.underline}]{.mark}** ground-based missile
[**[interceptors]{.mark}** are [located in the
state]{.mark}]{.underline}, whose high latitude enables earlier
detection of incoming threats. Recent [bilateral defense
agreements]{.underline} with all five Nordic countries and the accession
of Finland and Sweden to NATO, in 2023 and 2024, respectively, have
[strengthened collective defense in the Arctic]{.underline}. But
[[Washington]{.underline}]{.mark} has [**[neglected]{.mark}** its **[own
capabilities]{.mark}**]{.underline} in the region. U.S. military
[[officials]{.underline}]{.mark} often [[decry]{.underline}]{.mark} the
**[[lack of]{.underline}]{.mark}** port and [aviation
**[infrastructure]{.mark}**, icebreakers, satellites, sensors,
and]{.underline} cold-weather equipment and [training]{.underline} that
are [[necessary to defend]{.mark} Arctic
[territory]{.mark}.]{.underline}

As a reflection of DOD's interest in this technology, the 2019 National
Defense Authorization Act (NDAA) that Congress passed in August directed
the secretary of energy to report to Congress within a year on the
requirements and components needed for a pilot program to "site,
construct and operate at least one licensed micro-reactor that provides
resilience for national security infrastructure at a DOD or DOE
facility."

NEI's technical report, "Road Map for the Deployment of Micro-Reactors
for U.S. Department of Defense Domestic Installations," provides
guidance and input for the pilot program. The road map outlines the
timelines and challenges for deploying the first micro-reactor at a
domestic DOD facility and recommends actions to ensure its installment
by Dec. 31, 2027, as prescribed in the NDAA.

#### SMR's enhance military readiness.

**Chopra '23** \[Anil; Horace, Dorothy, and Katherine Johnson Professor
of Engineering Emeritus at UC Berkeley in Civil & Environmental

Besides reactors for electricity generation for civilian grids,

[for a longer time]{.underline}. In fact, nuclear power plants for
maritime applications began evolving in the United States in the 1940s.
The United States and Soviet Union have had nuclear powered submarines
since the early 1950s.1 [Unlike]{.underline} the
**[diesel]{.underline}**-electric powered
**[sub]{.underline}**marine**[s]{.underline}** [which have to surface

charging the batteries, **[sub]{.underline}**marine**[s]{.underline}**
[with nuclear propulsion are]{.underline} **[independent]{.underline}**

**life**]{.underline} [of]{.underline} typically around **[25

the relatively newer diesel submarines can at best remain submerged for
just a few days at slow speed. This figure could reduce to just a few
hours at high speeds.2 The design of marine-type reactors is quite
different from the electricity generation reactors used for the national
power grid supply.

This paper examines the military applications of nuclear power plants.
It examines their historical dimension, their present status and likely
future applications.

Small Nuclear power Reactors

The International Atomic Energy Agency (IAEA) defines 'small' as under
300 Megawatts electric (MWe), and up to about 700 MWe as 'medium'.3
[There is interest in **small** nuclear power **reactors**
for]{.underline} both **[military]{.underline}** and civil
[applications]{.underline}. For long, [the navy has been
using]{.underline} **[nuclear]{.underline}** powered aircraft
**[carriers]{.underline}** [and]{.underline}
**[sub]{.underline}**marine**[s]{.underline}**. [There is]{.underline}
also [interest for]{.underline} both **[military]{.underline}** and

plants]{.underline} for use [in]{.underline} **[remote

[intensive]{.underline}, especially for transmission. Experience from

[would also mean]{.underline} **[economy]{.underline}** [of]{.underline}
**[scale]{.underline}**. Modern [small reactors are]{.underline} being
designed to be **[modular]{.underline}**. Tere are also very small units
which are about 15 MWe, especially for remote communities.

Military Small Power Reactors

For long, the US Army has been using mobile and static [small
reactors]{.underline}. These were meant to [power]{.underline}
**[remote]{.underline}** [air/missile]{.underline} [defence radar
stations in]{.underline} **[Alaska]{.underline}**,
**[Greenland]{.underline}**, [and Antarctica, for providing
electricity]{.underline} and heating. A merchant ship called Liberty was
converted into the first experimental Floating Nuclear Power Plant
(FNPP) in mid-1960s.Te truck-mobile, and air-transportable plant was of
the size of a standard shipping container. It was operated in the Panama
Canal Zone from 1968-77.4

The US [armed forces are looking at]{.underline}
**[reducing]{.underline}** [power]{.underline} **[supply
vulnerabilities]{.underline}** [and operating]{.underline} [**costs**,
and]{.underline} also reducing [petroleum dependence]{.underline}. [Mini
Nuclear Power Plants]{.underline} (**[MNPPs]{.underline}**) [would be
**portable**]{.underline} by truck or large C-17 class aircraft [and
operate]{.underline} **[unrefuelled]{.underline}** [for]{.underline}
10-**[20 years]{.underline}**. More recently, [they have been looking
at]{.underline} mobile Small Modular Reactors ([**SMRs**)
for]{.underline} **[rapid response]{.underline}**
[scenarios]{.underline}. [These will be relevant in the case
of]{.underline} an electrical **[grid attack]{.underline}**
[or]{.underline} for [augmenting]{.underline} **[power]{.underline}**
[requirements]{.underline}. These would also be handy during
Humanitarian Assistance and Disaster Relief (HADR) operations. The
reactors should need minimal operating crew. The smaller ones, weighing
less than 40 tons, could generate around 10 MWe for around three years
without refuelling. Safety and reliability will be crucial. Fairly tight
installation and shutdown timelines have been specified by the
Department of Defence (DoD). The environmental impact has been factored
in. In March 2021, the DoD announced that "Project Pele5 " was on track,
and full-power testing of the mobile reactor was expected in 2023.
Outdoor mobile testing of the prototype micro-reactor is likely in 2024.
US firms, such as NuScale, are also working on a
**[floating]{.underline}** version of an **[SMR]{.underline}**. These
[could be used for]{.underline} the **[power]{.underline}**
[requirements of coastal areas]{.underline} when anchored close to the
shore. There are other SMR projects by Canadian and Japanese frms.
Rolls-Royce has been developing naval reactors since the 1950s, and is
also working on small test reactors.

#### Small-modular reactors solve\-\--and enable deployment of new tech.

Marcus **Nichol 18**, Executive Director, Nuclear Energy Institute;
\"Roadmap for the Deployment of Micro-Reactors for U.S. Department of
Defense Domestic Installations,\" Defense Technical Information Center,
10-4-2018, https://apps.dtic.mil/sti/citations/AD1083045. //TL

[**[Resilience]{.mark}** is a [primary requirement]{.mark} for
DoD]{.underline}. Resilience is defined in the 2018 NDAA as "the
[[ability to]{.underline}]{.mark} avoid,
**[[prepare]{.underline}]{.mark}** for, **[[minimize,
adapt]{.underline}]{.mark}** to, [[and **recover**
from]{.underline}]{.mark} anticipated and [[unanticipated]{.mark} energy
[disruptions]{.mark} in order to ensure energy]{.underline} availability
and reliability [sufficient]{.underline} to provide [for]{.underline}
mission assurance and [readiness]{.underline}, including task critical
assets and other mission essential operations related to readiness, and
to execute or rapidly reestablish mission essential requirements." The
2018 NDAA also defines energy security as "having assured access to
reliable supplies of energy and the ability to protect and deliver
sufficient energy to meet mission essential requirements.'' [Energy
resilience must be achieved in a manner that does not negatively impact
the mission]{.underline}. Generation sources that minimize usage of
land, air and water resources, including site access for fuel receipt
and storage, will enhance the installation's ability to focus on mission
critical activities.

[Currently]{.underline}, DoD [[installations **rely** ]{.mark}**almost
entirely** [on the grid]{.mark}, which **is [highly
vulnerable]{.mark}**]{.underline} to prolonged outage from a variety of
threats, [[placing]{.mark} critical [missions at **unacceptably high
risk**]{.mark}]{.underline} of extended disruption. Backup power is
often based on diesel generator sets with limited on-site fuel storage,
undersized for new Homeland defense missions, not prioritized to
critical loads and inadequate in duration and reliability. This is
[according to]{.underline} a Defense Science Board Task Force on
[DoD]{.underline} Energy Strategy that also [found]{.underline}
"critical [national security and Homeland defense missions are at an
unacceptably high risk of extended outage]{.underline} from failure of
the grid."9

DoD Instruction 4170.11, Installation Energy Management, provides policy
direction to ensure energy resilience at military installations. Each
military branch appears to address energy resilience differently, for
example the Army contextualizes resilience in terms of energy security,
while the Air Force contextualizes resilience in terms of energy
assurance. Efforts to enhance resilience also appear to focus along the
lines in which resilience has been contextualized. 10 While the detailed
scenario and capability requirements necessary to provide resilience at
DoD installations are not known and may be unique to each facility,
there are a number of considerations that appear to be universally
important. These include minimization of outages due to maintenance and
failures, being robust against natural and man-made threats, the ability
to quickly restore critical operations, and having secure access to the
source, delivery and on-site storage of fuel. More detailed requirements
for resilience for Air Force Installations are contained in a 2017 RAND
report.11

[[Micro-reactors offer]{.mark} the ability to **assure [energy
resilience]{.mark}**]{.underline} at DoD installations.
[Micro-reactors]{.underline} can [[operate]{.underline}]{.mark} 24 hours
a day, 7 days a week, **[[365 days a year]{.underline}]{.mark}**, and
are being [designed to operate [for **several years** without]{.mark}
the need to [shutdown]{.mark}]{.underline} for refueling. Micro-reactors
are being [[designed to protect against]{.mark} severe natural phenomena
as well as]{.underline} man-made [**[physical and cyber]{.mark}**
security **[threats]{.mark}**]{.underline}, and many are being designed
with the ability to operate in island-mode and include black-start
capabilities. Micro-reactors are also being designed to be able to
adjust output to meet changes in demand.

Micro-reactors will be able to provide heat and other products, such as
desalinated water and hydrogen that can meet the needs of DoD
installations. [[Micro-reactors]{.underline}]{.mark} can [also
**[enhance]{.mark}**]{.underline} DoD's [[use of **new
tech**]{.mark}**nologies**, [such as]{.mark}]{.underline} advanced
[[computing]{.mark}, "big data]{.underline}" analytics,
[[a]{.underline}]{.mark}rtificial [[i]{.underline}]{.mark}ntelligence,
[autonomy]{.underline}, [robotics, [directed energy, hypersonics, and
biotech]{.mark}]{.underline}nology. The very technologies that ensure
the U.S. will be able to fight and win the wars of the future.12

The [**air** lanes and **sea** lanes]{.underline} of the [Great Circle
Routes are [**heavily** trafficked]{.mark}]{.underline} by shipping

[[for]{.mark} their [economic **benefit**]{.mark}]{.underline}.

**[R]{.mark}**oute]{.underline} and the [**fabled** Northwest

[[parallels Russia's]{.mark} Arctic [coastline]{.mark}]{.underline}, as

**[S]{.underline}**mall **[M]{.underline}**odular
**[R]{.underline}**eactor**[s]{.underline}** [are]{.underline} a new

**flexible**, and **easier** to deploy]{.underline} than traditional

**[ideal]{.underline}** [for]{.underline} areas like [the]{.underline}

In the Arctic, **[energy reliability]{.underline}** [is]{.underline}
[**critical**. Military]{.underline} **[bases]{.underline}**
[need]{.underline} **[uninterrupted]{.underline}** [power for
operations]{.underline}, while towns require electricity and heat to
sustain life in subzero temperatures. [Renewable sources
like]{.underline} **[wind]{.underline}** [and]{.underline}
**[solar]{.underline}** [are]{.underline} **[inconsistent]{.underline}**
[in this environment, and]{.underline} **[diesel]{.underline}**

[**g**reen**h**ouse **g**ases]{.underline}. [SMRs]{.underline}, with

Canada's Energy Challenge in the Arctic

The Canadian [Arctic]{.underline} spans vast, sparsely populated
[territories]{.underline}, including Nunavut, the Northwest Territories,
and parts of Yukon. Many of its communities, often Indigenous, [rely on
diesel generators flown or shipped in at]{.underline} **[great
expense]{.underline}**. For example, a town like Iqaluit, with a
population of about 8,000, spends millions annually on fuel, and power
**[outages]{.underline}** [are]{.underline} [common during
storms]{.underline}. **[Military bases]{.underline}** like CFS Alert,
located on Ellesmere Island near the North Pole, [face]{.underline}
similar [issues]{.underline}, **[compounded]{.underline}** [by
their]{.underline} **[strategic importance]{.underline}** [in
monitoring]{.underline} **[Arctic sovereignty]{.underline}**.

[Diesel dependency is not just a]{.underline} [**financial
burden**---it's an environmental one. Transporting fuel
emits]{.underline} **[carbon]{.underline}** dioxide, [and]{.underline}
burning it [releases]{.underline} **[pollutants]{.underline}** [that
harm the]{.underline} **[fragile]{.underline}** [Arctic]{.underline}
**[ecosystem]{.underline}**. As Canada aims for net-zero emissions by
2050, replacing diesel with cleaner alternatives is a priority. [SMRs
could]{.underline} meet this need by [deliver]{.underline}ing
**[consistent]{.underline}** [power]{.underline} and heat,
[reducing]{.underline} both **[costs]{.underline}** [and]{.underline}
**[emissions]{.underline}**.

Canada's Push for SMRs

Canada has a strong foundation in nuclear technology, with over six
decades of experience through its CANDU reactors. Building on this
expertise, the government launched the SMR Roadmap in 2018, followed by
the SMR Action Plan in 2020. These initiatives involve collaboration
with industry, utilities, and Indigenous groups to explore SMR
deployment. The goal is twofold: decarbonize remote regions and spur
economic growth.

The Arctic is a key focus. Natural Resources Canada sees SMRs as a way
to power northern communities and support industries like mining, which
is vital for extracting minerals such as rare earth elements. For
instance, [a single]{.underline} **[SMR]{.underline}** [could
supply]{.underline} **[electricity]{.underline}** and district heating
to a town of several thousand, **[slashing]{.underline}** energy
**[costs]{.underline}** [and improving **reliability**]{.underline}.
[Military bases could]{.underline} also [benefit, gaining
energy]{.underline} **[independence]{.underline}** [and
reducing]{.underline} **[vulnerability]{.underline}** [to
**fuel**]{.underline} [supply **disruptions**]{.underline}.

#### Arctic military bases are limited by fuel availability challenges and costs.

**[scarce]{.underline}**. Unlike in other military theaters, [Arctic

[**self-sufficiency**, relying on prepositioned]{.underline}

**[challenge]{.underline}** as [ice, snow and a lack of]{.underline}

**[expensive]{.underline}** [to operate in]{.underline}.

Several times in the course of the past few years, that access to
uninterrupted power has been challenged, both by increasingly severe
weather and bad actors in cyberspace. Experts are hoping these incidents
will be a wake-up call for the Air Force and the public in general to
make sure their power systems are resilient enough to bounce back from
crises.

ENERGY IN A MODERNIZED AIR FORCE

"[Every]{.underline} Department of [the Air Force mission starts and
ends on an installation]{.underline}," wrote Mark A. Correll, deputy
assistant secretary of the Air Force for environment, safety, and
infrastructure, in May 19 testimony to the Senate Appropriations
subcommittee on military construction. "[Installations are weapon
systems]{.underline}."

As new technologies emerge and the Department of the Air Force
modernizes, more and more missions can---or must be---accomplished or
supported from bases within the U.S., especially for a service like the
Space Force.

"Nowadays, \[with\] intel, data-driven operations, UAVs---the
installations in the United States and the global infrastructure that
supports the forward deployed soldiers and Airmen and the planes and the
infrastructure are actually operational assets, not strategic," said
retired Army Maj. Gen. John G. Ferrari, now a nonresident senior fellow
at the American Enterprise Institute. "They are part of the
warfight---for better or worse."

As part of the warfight, [[installation's]{.mark} strengths and
[**vulnerabilities** are amplified]{.mark}]{.underline}, especially in
the context of a modernizing Air Force. One [[key]{.mark} vulnerability
[is]{.mark}]{.underline} USAF's **[[reliance on
electricity]{.underline}]{.mark}**.

"We are just simply more and more connected as a society," Wu said. "And
those connection points create new alliances and dependencies on
electricity---in particular, communications networks, but on critical
infrastructure generally."

Disrupt any one of those connection points in a particular Air Force
mission, and it "can have cascading impacts on other parts of the
mission that may be miles away," the service's Installation Energy
Strategic Plan, released in January 2021, acknowledged. And the threats
to those connection points are becoming both more frequent and more
potent, experts say.

SEVERE WEATHER

In particular, [severe weather events]{.underline}, which scientists
believe [are becoming more frequent]{.underline} due to climate change,
have [wreaked havoc and caused billions of dollars in
damages]{.underline} as of late. There was the near-total destruction of
Tyndall Air Force Base, Fla., by Hurricane Michael in 2018, the massive
floods at Offutt Air Force Base, Neb., in 2019, and most recently,
Winter Storm Uri in early 2021, which impacted 28 Department of the Air
Force installations across the U.S., including several that experienced
interruptions to power or water service.

[One of the bases affected]{.underline} during the winter storm was
Minot Air Force Base, N.D. In his written testimony to the Senate
Appropriations subcommittee, Correll said that the storm [caused a
portion of the base's missile field to lose power]{.underline} and rely
on backup generators to operate. It's [not the first time]{.underline}
Minot's [nuclear silos have been impacted by
weather]{.underline}---severe flooding in 2011 prevented access to seven
launch facilities.

Such events take money, manpower, and time to recover, and missions can
be affected as equipment is damaged or units must relocate.

The threat of severe weather and climate change is particularly acute in
certain areas, said David R. Haines, Senior Fellow for Climate Security
at the American Security Project.

"Those \[bases\] that are most at risk tend to be those who are out
West, where there is potential damage from wildfires and droughts, and
those that are on the coast, where they can be damaged by rising sea
levels, floods---including sunny day flooding---and, obviously,
hurricanes," Haines said. "Talking about Tyndall, that's probably the
poster child for climate damage---\$3 billion worth of damage."

As required in the fiscal 2020 National Defense Authorization Act
(NDAA), the [Air Force conducted initial assessments]{.underline} of the
threats posed by severe weather and other natural disasters [at more
than 90 installations,]{.underline} said Secretary of the Air Force
spokeswoman Sarah Fiocco. As part of those assessments, the Air Force
categorized the individual risk level from each climate threat to each
base.

"Roughly [10 percent of the hazards identified as risks were categorized
as Extremely High or High,]{.underline} 25 percent as Medium, and the
remainder as Low," Fiocco said.

Those [assessments]{.underline} have [already made an
impact]{.underline}. The Air Force acting [assistant secretary for
energy,]{.underline} installations, and environment, Jennifer L. Miller,
[told]{.underline} a [congressional panel]{.underline} in July that
rebuilding [plans]{.underline} at Offutt and Tyndall have been [adjusted
to account for the threats]{.underline}, and moving forward, each base
will update its master plans with the results of the assessment within a
planned five-year cycle.

CYBER THREATS

[Nature isn't the only threat,]{.underline} however. As the military has
developed the capacity to strike globally from its home bases,
[adversaries and bad actors]{.underline} have [gained the ability to
reachback via the internet]{.underline}.

"We've now spent 70 years building the infrastructure \[in the U.S.\]
under the assumption that nothing can happen to it," said Ferrari, who
served as a branch chief for contingency operations for the Joint Chiefs
of Staff. "And in many ways, our belief in that northern, southern,
eastern, and western moats, if you will, is our Maginot line. It gives
you the illusion of defense, and we haven't realized that really, the
borders, they don't matter anymore."

France's Maginot Line was built as a bulwark against German aggression
after World War I and was expected to be unbreachable. A generation
later, Germans invaded by another route, and France surrendered in days.

When the [[Colonial Pipeline]{.underline}]{.mark} was [shut down
earlier]{.underline} this year by a ransomware attack that [[caused
**fuel shortages**]{.underline}]{.mark} and panic up and down the East
Coast, the [Defense [Department got a glimpse of]{.mark} what that
[risk]{.mark} looks like]{.underline}. While there was no impact on the
military's mission capability at the time, Ferrari said it [showed the
**risk [of an "existential threat]{.mark}**]{.underline}," especially
[when combined with other attacks or crises.]{.underline}

"If somebody's going to the trouble of taking out your electric grid,
and hey, by the way, there's no electricity, and the fuel pumps, the
pipelines don't work, and you can't move fuel, and now you've got
Colonial Pipeline and now you don't have fuel, so how long are your
generators going to last?" Ferrari said. "The outcome becomes
exponentially worse when you're dealing with both of those at the same
time."

'POSSIBLY MONTHS'

In 2016, then-Air Force Secretary Deborah Lee James issued Air Force
Policy Directive 90-17, establishing a "framework for energy and water
resources management." James made it Air Force policy that every
installation should be able to "power any infrastructure identified as
critical to the performance of mission essential functions independent
of the utility grid" for at least seven days or until the mission could
be relocated.

Five years later, the office for Energy, Installations, and Environment
released its Installation Energy Strategic Plan, acknowledging that many
of its planning scenarios were based on energy outages lasting one to
seven days.

However, in the context of increasingly severe and potentially long-term
threats, especially ones that can build off one another, experts and
officials are expanding their time frames.

"Folks who are operationally planning in the Air Force and who are aware
of, and really cognizant of, the potential risks and threats that the
Air Force and the rest of our nation face, I think, are thinking on the
weeks and possibly months scale," said Wu, who now runs Converge
Strategies, aimed at promoting energy security with military and
civilian partners. "Because that's real."

Air Force Instruction 10-208 Continuity of Operations (COOP), issued in
2018, establishes guidelines for continuity of operations plans and
requires command-wide COOP programs to consider the possibility of
primary power outages lasting "seven, 30, or greater than 90 days."

[[In]{.mark} the [context of a few hours]{.mark}]{.underline} or days,
[diesel-powered [generators are]{.mark}]{.underline} a
[[viable]{.underline}]{.mark} backup option; but [for long-term outages,
fuel can become scarce]{.underline}.

"Backing up critical facilities with [**diesel** generators]{.underline}
is [[simply **not up to the challenge** in]{.mark} the [long
term]{.mark}]{.underline} of the widespread power disruption scenarios
that we're now considering," Wu said.

Indeed, the service's Installation Energy Strategic Plan does account
for the possibility of more long-term disruptions. Both natural
disasters and cyber attacks are listed as potential causes for outages
that could last for upward of three months. In such scenarios, other
courses of action will have to be employed.

POTENTIAL SOLUTIONS

As part of the fiscal year 2021 National Defense Authorization Act,
Congress required the Secretary of Defense to ensure by fiscal 2030 that
99.9 percent of the energy load required for critical missions on
installations be available.

Within that mandate, though, was no one particular way to accomplish
that goal that the department will have to follow. That's as it should
be, experts agreed.

"It's definitely got to be more tailored," Haines said, "which is, I
believe, why the NDAA put the stipulation in that says you need to make
your own energy on the base, but they didn't say, 'Here's how you have
to make it,' because in different places you're gonna have different
advantages."

As an example, Ferrari cited Marine Corps Air Station Miramar in
Southern California which has harnessed methane gas from a nearby
landfill to install a microgrid that allows the base to operate even
when blackouts strike the area.

Microgrids---small energy systems connected to the larger utility grid
but capable of disconnecting and running on their own when needed---are
a key part of the energy resilience puzzle, many believe. A number of
Air Force installations have already either installed microgrid controls
to their energy systems or have plans to do so.

Another [[potential **solution is**]{.mark}]{.underline} small modular
reactors (**[[SMR]{.underline}]{.mark}**)---[nuclear power plants
reduced in size [to be]{.mark}]{.underline} more [[compact, less
costly]{.underline}]{.mark}, [potentially]{.underline} even
[[portable]{.underline}]{.mark}. President J. Donald Trump issued an
[executive order]{.underline} in the final days of his term directing
the Defense Department [to **[promote SMR r]{.mark}esearch [and
d]{.mark}evelopment** [for national defense]{.mark}]{.underline}, and
Miller told a congressional panel that she has "[great interest" in the
idea.]{.underline}

"We're just getting underway on the pilot program but we're excited to
see what capabilities that provides us for our CONUS installations and
then the potential to have those be mobile for other locations," Miller
said.

#### Arctic Airpower underpins [deterrence]{.underline} AND allied [cooperation]{.underline}.

At the end of the Cold War, many considered the Arctic to be a secure

many co-managed with Canada, that cross the top of North America and
form an essential part of protecting the U.S. and Canada from missile
and bomber attacks. Northern bases are key staging locations, allowing
aircraft to quickly reach any location in the Northern Hemisphere. The
Air Force also recently upgraded critical space-surveillance assets in
Thule, Greenland, the northernmost U.S. base in the world. Meanwhile we
continue to train and equip for cold weather operations, managing the
Defense Department's oldest polar survival school and retaining the
capacity to land on ice with unique, ski-equipped aircraft.

Completing these missions is not without challenges. [The region poses
**difficulties** with extreme]{.underline} cold and unpredictable
**[weather]{.underline}**, [short **construction** seasons, and
extended]{.underline} periods of **[darkness]{.underline}**, in addition
to aurora and space weather phenomena [that inhibit]{.underline} radio
and satellite **[communication]{.underline}**.

These demands make the Arctic a region where [**[alliances]{.mark}** and
partnerships [are]{.mark}]{.underline} all the more [**[vital]{.mark}**.
In addition to]{.underline} our enduring partnership with
**[Canada]{.underline}**, the Air Force is looking at opportunities to
[expand relationships with **other** Arctic allie**s**]{.underline} ---
especially through exercises --- by [sharing]{.underline} weather,
communications and reconnaissance [**data**, as well as
trading]{.underline} operational [best **practices**.]{.underline}

The casualties in the Arctic during World War II highlighted the
**[[perils]{.underline}]{.mark}** that [[result from]{.mark} a [lack of
**readiness**]{.mark}]{.underline} for operating in extreme conditions.
Many are calling for increased attention, including Congress. The 2019
National Defense Authorization Act requires an updated Defense
Department Arctic Strategy outlining the roles and missions of each
military service in the region. World events and history suggest it's
time to move out smartly to protect our vital national interests.

The decay began after the fall of the Soviet Union in 1991, when the

fighters and bombers to the core.

\<\<TEXT CONDENSED, NONE OMITTED\>\>

To avert this outcome, modernization is essential. This includes: Buying
more F-35 and B-21 aircraft as rapidly as possible Developing and
fielding autonomous, uncrewed Collaborative Combat Aircraft (CCA)
Building the Next Generation Air Dominance (NGAD) family of systems
Investing in modern munitions in volume Fixing the chronic pilot
shortfall Stop shortchanging operations accounts so that personnel are
adequately trained, and mission equipment is sustained at a high level
of readiness. A credible force can deter war, but a hollow force---one
that lacks the capacity to fight a peer adversary---invites risk.
Adversaries know when America is weak and will opportunistically push
against our interests. However, modernization requires resources, and
that funding is in short supply given new budget caps, continued high
inflation, and the sheer scale of modernization necessary to reset
decades of neglect. The factors risk seeing an already a fragile set of
Air Force combat airpower capabilities grow more precarious. The Air
Force reduced its fiscal 2025 procurement budget request by \$1.6
billion versus fiscal 2024, because the resources simply were not
available in the topline dictated by the Pentagon. According to experts,
the defense budget caps amount to a \$47 billion cut to the Department
of Defense in fiscal 2025 versus previously planned toplines. Nor are
things set to improve any time soon. Secretary of the Air Force Frank
Kendall explained: "You're going to see our five-year plan ... and what
you're going to see is that life gets a lot harder as we get past '25."
Given what is at stake, Congress must repeal the budget caps and
prioritize Air Force modernization. The joint force simply is not viable
without what Air Force fighters and bombers bring to the fight. Nor can
these deferred bills be ignored forever. As Senate defense
appropriations subcommittee Chairman Sen. Jon Tester (D-Mont.) knows
well, the cost of postponing necessary investments is always higher:
"Either pay me now or pay me later," he has said. "It's going to cost a
hell of a lot more later." Ranking member of the Senate Armed Services
Committee Sen. Roger Wicker (R-Miss.) is calling for higher defense
spending. "Our current defense investment does not meet the moment," he
said, releasing a plan that deserves serious bipartisan attention. "The
emerging Axis of Aggressors is undermining U.S. interests across the
globe." On top of more funding, the Department of Defense also needs to
embrace cost per effect analysis to ensure money received is directed
towards achieving best value. It comes down to investing in systems that
deliver the greatest effect at the lowest cost, thus buying more defense
capability and capacity for each dollar invested.
